Logic and Technical Terms
beginner
5 min
Definition of Force (Quwwa) and Four Types
Sanusi defines quwwa and classifies four forces.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of quwwa as an attribute that is a principle of change in another, and the classification of forces into animal, celestial, plant, and elemental based on intention and diversity of effects.

Core Idea
Force is a principle of change in another; forces are classified by intention and effect variation.

Sanusi opens his discussion of qudra by first defining the broader concept of quwwa (force). He gives a precise philosophical definition and then classifies all forces into four distinct types. This classification is foundational because it determines which forces qualify as 'power' (qudra) in the theological sense. Read carefully to understand the criteria he uses.

Guided Path
1
Read the formal definition of quwwa
Start with the Arabic: القوة وهي صفة تكون مبدأ التغير في آخر من حيث هو آخر. Note the key elements: attribute, principle of change, in another, inasmuch as it is another.
The definition emphasizes relation to another entity.
2
Identify the two criteria for classification
Sanusi classifies forces based on two binary criteria: accompaniment of intention (qasd) and diversity of effects (ikhtilāf al-āthār). Identify these in the text.
These criteria reappear in the discussion of qudra.
3
List the four types with their descriptions
Find the four forces: animal (with both intention and diverse effects), celestial (with intention but uniform effects), plant (without intention but diverse effects), and elemental (neither intention nor diverse effects).
Only animal force is called qudra by consensus.
4
Note what is excluded
Sanusi excludes substantial forms and souls because they are substances, not accidents. Forces here are considered as attributes (accidents).
This limits the discussion to accidental forces.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الأول تعريف القدرة وبيانها في مباحث : المبحث الأول القوة وهي صفة تكون مبدأ التغير في آخر من حيث هو آخر ، إما مقارنة للقصد أو لا . وكل منها إما مختلفة الآثار أو لا، فالأولى القوة الحيوانية والثانية الفلكية والثالثة النباتية، والرابعة العنصرية، وليس الكلام في الصور النوعية والنفوس لأنها من قبيل الجواهر والمعتبر في كون القوة قدرة إما مقارنة القصد أو اختلاف الآثار، ولهذا قيل: صفة تؤثر وفق الإرادة، أو يكون مبدأ لأفعال مختلفة، فالمشتملة عليهما قدرة اتفاقاً كالقوة الحيوانية، والخالية عنهما ليست بقدرة اتفاقاً كالقوى العنصرية، والمشتملة على إحداهما فقط مختلف فيها كالقوى الفلكية والنباتية والمراد استعداد التأثير ليشمل القدرة الحادثة على رأينا . ولهذا قيل صفة بها يتمكن من الفعل والترك) . هي مبدأ التغير من قصد لفظ القدرة يقال للصفة التي بها يتمكن الحيوان من مزاولة أفعال شاقة، ويقابلها الضعف وقد يقال لصفة المؤثرية، فيفسر بصفة شيء في خر من حيث هو آخر فقوله في آخر إشعار بوجوب التغاير بين المؤثر والمتأثر ، وقيد الحيثية إشعار بأنه يكفي التغاير بحسب الاعتبار كالطبيب يعالج نفسه فيؤثر من حيث إنه عالم بالصناعة ويتأثر من حيث إنه جسم ينفعل عما يلاقيه من الدواء، وهذا بالنظر إلى ظاهر الإطلاق، وإلا فعند التحقيق التأثير للنفس والتأثر للبدن ولو مثل بالمعالج نفسه في تهذيب الأخلاق وتبديل الملكات لكان أقرب ثم القوة التي هي وصف المؤثرية إما أن تكون مع وشعور بأثرها أو لا وعلى التقديرين فإما أن تكون آثارها مختلفة أو لا، فالأولى الصفة المؤثرة مع القصد والشعور واختلاف الآثار والأفعال هي القوة الحيوانية المسماة بالقدرة والثانية وهي القوة المؤثرية على سبيل القصد والشعور لكن على نهج واحد من غير اختلاف في آثارها وهي القوة الفلكية، والثالثة وهي المبدأ لآثار وأفعال مختلفة لا على سبيل القصد والشعور)(۱). هي القوة النباتية، والرابعة وهي مبدأ الأثر على نهج واحد بدون القصد والشعور هي القوة العنصرية، وهذه كلها من أقسام العرض على ما يشعر به لفظ الصفة وهي المبادىء القريبة للأفعال وإما أن لكل منها أو لبعضها مبادىء من قبيل الجواهر تسمى بالصور النوعية والنفوس فذلك بحث آخر، وقد ينازع في إثبات القوى الفلكية والنباتية إذا أريد بها غير النفوس والصور إذا تقرر هذا فنقول : وهي اعتبر بعضهم في كون القوة قدرة مقارنتها للقصد والشعور ففسر القدرة
First Investigation: Definition of qudra and its exposition in investigations: First Investigation: al-quwwa, which is an attribute (sifa) that is a principle of change in another insofar as it is another, either accompanying intention (qasd) or not. And each of them either has different effects or not. The first is the animal force, the second the celestial force, the third the plant force, and the fourth the elemental force. There is no discussion regarding substantial forms (suwar naw'iyya) and souls (nufus) because they are of the kind of substances (jawhar). What is considered in a force being qudra is either accompaniment of intention or difference of effects. For this it was said: an attribute that effects according to will, or is a principle of different actions. That which includes both is qudra by agreement, like the animal force; that which is devoid of both is not qudra by agreement, like the elemental forces; that which includes only one of them is disputed, like the celestial and plant forces. And the intended meaning is the readiness (isti'dad) for effect, to include temporal qudra according to our view. For this it was said: an attribute by which one is able to do and to leave. It is a principle of change with intention. The word qudra is said of the attribute by which the animal is able to perform difficult actions, and its opposite is weakness. And it may be said of the attribute of effectivity (mu'aththiriyya), so it is interpreted as an attribute of a thing in another insofar as it is another. His statement 'in another' indicates the necessity of distinction between the effecter (mu'aththir) and the affected (mu'aththar), and the qualification of 'insofar as' indicates that distinction according to consideration suffices, like the doctor treating himself: he effects insofar as he is knowledgeable in the art, and is affected insofar as he is a body that is passive to what he meets of medicine. This is with respect to the apparent absolute sense; otherwise, upon investigation, the effect is from the soul and the passivity is from the body. Had he exemplified with the one treating himself in the refinement of character and the change of dispositions, it would have been more fitting. Then al-quwwa, which is the description of effectivity, is either with awareness of its effect or not, and in both cases either its effects are different or not. The first: the influencing attribute with intention and awareness and difference of effects and actions is the animal force called al-qudra. The second: the influencing force by way of intention and awareness but in a single pattern without difference in its effects, and it is the celestial force. The third: the principle for different effects and actions not by way of intention and awareness: it is the plant force. The fourth: the principle of effect in a single pattern without intention and awareness: it is the elemental force. All these are among the divisions of accident as indicated by the word 'attribute', and they are the proximate principles of actions. And if each or some of them have principles from the kind of substances called substantial forms and souls, that is another investigation. One may dispute the affirmation of celestial and plant forces if by them is meant other than souls and forms. Now that this is established, we say: And some considered in a force being qudra its accompaniment of intention and awareness, so he defined al-qudra [as such].
Source pages p. 347

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification of force (quwwa) as it relates to power (qudra). It begins with a formal definition: al-quwwa is an attribute (sifa) that serves as the principle of change in another entity, insofar as that entity is other than itself. This change may or may not accompany intention (qasd). The author then classifies forces into four types based on two criteria: whether the force accompanies intention, and whether its effects are varied. The first type accompanies intention and produces varied effects – this is the animal force (al-quwwa al-hayawaniyya). The second accompanies intention but produces uniform effects – the celestial force (al-quwwa al-falakiyya). The third does not accompany intention but produces varied effects – the plant force (al-quwwa al-nabatiyya). The fourth has neither intention nor varied effects – the elemental force (al-quwwa al-'unsuriyya). The passage notes that substantial forms and souls are not discussed here because they are substances, not accidents. Then it addresses what qualifies a force as qudra: it requires either accompaniment of intention or variability of effects. If both are present (animal force), it is unanimously considered qudra. If neither (elemental), it is unanimously not qudra. If only one (celestial or plant), there is difference of opinion. The author also clarifies that 'isti'dad al-ta'thir' (readiness for effect) is meant to include temporal qudra. A second definition is given: qudra is an attribute by which one is able to act or refrain. The passage then explores the phrase 'attribute of a thing in another insofar as it is another' to emphasize the distinction between the effecter and the affected, using the example of a doctor treating himself. It reiterates the fourfold classification with more detail, labeling the animal force specifically as qudra. It notes that these forces are all accidents and proximate principles of actions, but each may have deeper principles (substantial forms or souls) which is a separate topic. Finally, it mentions that some scholars consider the accompaniment of intention and awareness as the condition for calling a force qudra.

Key Terms
قوة
قدرة
قصد
فلكية
نباتية
عنصرية
حيوانية
صفة
مبدأ التغير
اختلاف الآثار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All forces (quwwa) are considered powers (qudra).
Correction: Only forces that accompany intention or produce diverse effects qualify as qudra; elemental forces are universally excluded, and celestial/plant forces are disputed.

Checkpoint
Which type of force is unanimously considered qudra according to the passage?
A) The celestial force
B) The plant force
C) The animal force
D) The elemental force

Key Takeaway
Force is defined as a principle of change in another, and only the animal force—combining intention and diverse effects—is universally recognized as qudra.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have animal force (qudra) with intention and diverse effects. When was the last time you used this force for an action you now regret? What does the requirement of intention and awareness imply about your responsibility for that action? Will you seek forgiveness and amend your ways today?
?
Celestial forces operate with intention but uniform effects—they never deviate. In contrast, your animal force can vary. Which of your daily habits are so consistent that they resemble celestial uniformity, and which are chaotic? How can you intentionally bring more consistency to your worship and character?
?
The passage distinguishes forces that are qudra from those that are not. Do you live as though your actions are merely natural forces without moral weight? What would change if you fully embraced that your animal force, with its intention and diverse effects, makes you answerable to Allah for every choice? Write one concrete step you will take this week to align your actions with that accountability.

Definition of Qudra and Its Two Meanings
Al-Taftazani defines qudra and explains its two usages with the condition of effectivity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of qudra as an attribute of effectivity, its two usages (animal ability and attribute of effectivity), and the logical condition of distinction between effector and affected.

Core Idea
Qudra is the attribute by which an agent is enabled to act, requiring distinction between effector and affected.

Al-Taftazani begins by establishing what qudra means in the kalam tradition. He distinguishes between its common usage for animal strength and its technical meaning as the attribute of effectivity, which involves a relationship between two distinct entities. In this first part, he unpacks the definition and clarifies the key condition of distinction, using the physician treating himself as an illustration.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Notice the initial clarification about differing powers and the intended meaning of disposition for effect.
2
Identify the two definitions
The passage gives two usages for qudra: one for animal ability and one for attribute of effectivity. Locate both.
3
Understand the condition
Note the requirement of distinction between effector and affected, and how distinction in consideration suffices.
The physician example illustrates this.
4
Reflect on the example
Analyze how the physician treating himself shows that the same entity can be effector and affected under different aspects.
In reality, the soul effects and the body is affected.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فقط مختلف فيها كالقوى الفلكية ،والنباتية والمراد استعداد التأثير ليشمل القدرة الحادثة على رأينا . ولهذا قيل صفة بها يتمكن من الفعل والترك . هي مبدأ التغير من قصد . لفظ القدرة يقال للصفة التي بها يتمكن الحيوان من مزاولة أفعال شاقة، ويقابلها الضعف وقد يقال لصفة المؤثرية، فيفسر بصفة شيء في خر من حيث هو آخر فقوله في آخر إشعار بوجوب التغاير بين المؤثر والمتأثر ، وقيد الحيثية إشعار بأنه يكفي التغاير بحسب الاعتبار كالطبيب يعالج نفسه فيؤثر من حيث إنه عالم بالصناعة ويتأثر من حيث إنه جسم ينفعل عما يلاقيه من الدواء، وهذا بالنظر إلى ظاهر الإطلاق، وإلا فعند التحقيق التأثير للنفس والتأثر للبدن ولو مثل بالمعالج نفسه في تهذيب الأخلاق وتبديل الملكات لكان أقرب ثم القوة التي هي وصف المؤثرية إما أن تكون مع وشعور بأثرها أو لا وعلى التقديرين فإما أن تكون آثارها مختلفة أو لا، فالأولى الصفة المؤثرة مع القصد والشعور واختلاف الآثار والأفعال هي القوة الحيوانية المسماة بالقدرة والثانية وهي القوة المؤثرية على سبيل القصد والشعور لكن على نهج واحد من غير اختلاف في آثارها وهي القوة الفلكية، والثالثة وهي المبدأ لآثار وأفعال مختلفة لا على سبيل القصد والشعور (۱) هي القوة النباتية، والرابعة وهي مبدأ الأثر على نهج واحد بدون القصد والشعور هي القوة العنصرية، وهذه كلها من أقسام العرض على ما يشعر به لفظ الصفة وهي المبادىء القريبة للأفعال وإما أن لكل منها أو لبعضها مبادىء من قبيل الجواهر تسمى بالصور النوعية والنفوس فذلك بحث آخر
Only [powers] that differ in them, like the celestial and vegetative powers, and the intended meaning is the disposition for effect to include the originated capability according to our view. And for this it is said: [qudra is] an attribute by which one is enabled to perform an action or leave it. It is the principle of change by intention. The term qudra is used for the attribute by which an animal is enabled to undertake strenuous actions, and its opposite is weakness. And it may be used for the attribute of effectivity, and [then] it is interpreted as the attribute of a thing in another insofar as it is other. So his saying "in another" indicates the necessity of distinction between the effector and the affected, and the qualification of "insofar as" indicates that distinction in consideration suffices, like the physician treating himself: he effects insofar as he is knowledgeable in the art, and is affected insofar as he is a body that is acted upon by what he encounters of medicine. This is with respect to the apparent usage; otherwise, in reality the effect belongs to the soul and the being-affected to the body. And if he had exemplified by the one treating himself in refinement of morals and change of character traits, it would have been closer. Then the force that is the attribute of effectivity is either with intention and awareness of its effect or not; and in both cases, either its effects are diverse or not. The first—the effective attribute with intention and awareness and diversity of effects and actions—is the animal force called qudra. The second—the effective force by way of intention and awareness but on a single course with no diversity in its effects—is the celestial force. The third—the principle of diverse effects and actions not by way of intention and awareness—is the vegetative force. The fourth—the principle of effect on a single course without intention and awareness—is the elemental force. And all of these are categories of accident as implied by the word "attribute", and they are the proximate principles of actions. But whether each or some of them have principles from the category of substances called specific forms and souls—that is another discussion.
Source pages p. 348

Commentary
This passage defines qudra (capability/power) and classifies forces. It begins by clarifying that the discussion involves differing powers such as celestial and vegetative, and the intended meaning is 'disposition for effect' to include the created capability. Then it gives a standard definition: qudra is an attribute by which the agent is able to perform or leave an action. It is also called the principle of change by intention. The author notes that 'qudra' is used both for animal ability to perform strenuous actions (opposite: weakness) and for the attribute of effectivity (effecting change in another). For the latter, it is defined as an attribute of a thing in another insofar as it is other, requiring distinction between effector and affected, but distinction in consideration (like the physician treating himself) suffices. In reality, the effect belongs to soul and being-affected to body. Then forces are classified by two criteria: presence of intention/awareness, and diversity of effects. Four types emerge: (1) animal force—with intention, awareness, and diverse effects (this is called qudra); (2) celestial force—with intention and awareness but no diversity of effects; (3) vegetative force—without intention/awareness but with diverse effects; (4) elemental force—without intention/awareness and without diversity of effects. All these are accidental attributes (a'rad) and are proximate principles of actions. The passage ends by noting that whether they have substance-level principles (specific forms, souls) is another topic.

Key Terms
قدرة (qudra)
صفة المؤثرية (attribute of effectivity)
قصد (intention)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Qudra only refers to God's power.
Correction: The passage clarifies that qudra can refer to originated (created) capability as well, specifically mentioning 'al-qudra al-haditha' (the originated capability).

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what condition is necessary for an attribute to be considered 'effectivity'?
A) The effector and affected must be distinct entities.
B) The effector must be animate.
C) The effect must be physical.
D) The effector must intend the action.

Key Takeaway
Qudra is an attribute of effectivity requiring distinction between the one who acts and that which undergoes the action.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you fail to change a bad habit, do you blame lack of 'qudra' as ability, or do you recognize that you have the attribute of effectivity (influence over yourself) but simply choose not to act? Which definition of qudra applies to your situation, and what does that reveal about your responsibility?
?
The physician treats himself using his knowledge, affecting his own body. Where in your spiritual life are you both the doctor and the patient? What knowledge do you possess but fail to apply to yourself?
?
Qudra is defined as the principle of change by intention. Can you name one area of your character you intend to change but have not yet moved? What would it take for you to exercise your qudra in that area, starting today?

Logic and Technical Terms

Classification of Forces: Animal, Celestial, Vegetative, Elemental
Al-Taftazani classifies four types of forces based on intention/awareness and diversity of effects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how forces are classified according to presence of intention/awareness and diversity of effects, and identify the four resulting types.

Core Idea
Forces are categorized by whether they involve intention and whether their effects are diverse, yielding four distinct types.

After defining qudra as an attribute of effectivity, al-Taftazani now explains how this attribute varies in different beings. He uses two criteria—intention with awareness, and diversity of effects—to produce a fourfold classification of forces. This gives a comprehensive view of how different forces operate in the cosmos, from animals to celestial bodies, plants, and inanimate elements.

Guided Path
1
Read the classification passage
Read the Arabic and translation, noting the two binary criteria used: presence of intention/awareness, and diversity of effects.
2
Identify the four types
List the four forces in order: animal, celestial, vegetative, elemental. For each, note the presence/absence of intention and diversity.
3
Understand the defining characteristics
Compare the animal force (with intention, diverse effects) to the celestial (with intention, single effect), etc.
Notice that the animal force is called 'qudra' specifically.
4
Reflect on the broader context
Note the author's comment that these are accidental attributes and proximate principles of actions, and that the substance-level principles are a separate discussion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فقط مختلف فيها كالقوى الفلكية ،والنباتية والمراد استعداد التأثير ليشمل القدرة الحادثة على رأينا . ولهذا قيل صفة بها يتمكن من الفعل والترك . هي مبدأ التغير من قصد . لفظ القدرة يقال للصفة التي بها يتمكن الحيوان من مزاولة أفعال شاقة، ويقابلها الضعف وقد يقال لصفة المؤثرية، فيفسر بصفة شيء في خر من حيث هو آخر فقوله في آخر إشعار بوجوب التغاير بين المؤثر والمتأثر ، وقيد الحيثية إشعار بأنه يكفي التغاير بحسب الاعتبار كالطبيب يعالج نفسه فيؤثر من حيث إنه عالم بالصناعة ويتأثر من حيث إنه جسم ينفعل عما يلاقيه من الدواء، وهذا بالنظر إلى ظاهر الإطلاق، وإلا فعند التحقيق التأثير للنفس والتأثر للبدن ولو مثل بالمعالج نفسه في تهذيب الأخلاق وتبديل الملكات لكان أقرب ثم القوة التي هي وصف المؤثرية إما أن تكون مع وشعور بأثرها أو لا وعلى التقديرين فإما أن تكون آثارها مختلفة أو لا، فالأولى الصفة المؤثرة مع القصد والشعور واختلاف الآثار والأفعال هي القوة الحيوانية المسماة بالقدرة والثانية وهي القوة المؤثرية على سبيل القصد والشعور لكن على نهج واحد من غير اختلاف في آثارها وهي القوة الفلكية، والثالثة وهي المبدأ لآثار وأفعال مختلفة لا على سبيل القصد والشعور (۱) هي القوة النباتية، والرابعة وهي مبدأ الأثر على نهج واحد بدون القصد والشعور هي القوة العنصرية، وهذه كلها من أقسام العرض على ما يشعر به لفظ الصفة وهي المبادىء القريبة للأفعال وإما أن لكل منها أو لبعضها مبادىء من قبيل الجواهر تسمى بالصور النوعية والنفوس فذلك بحث آخر
Only [powers] that differ in them, like the celestial and vegetative powers, and the intended meaning is the disposition for effect to include the originated capability according to our view. And for this it is said: [qudra is] an attribute by which one is enabled to perform an action or leave it. It is the principle of change by intention. The term qudra is used for the attribute by which an animal is enabled to undertake strenuous actions, and its opposite is weakness. And it may be used for the attribute of effectivity, and [then] it is interpreted as the attribute of a thing in another insofar as it is other. So his saying "in another" indicates the necessity of distinction between the effector and the affected, and the qualification of "insofar as" indicates that distinction in consideration suffices, like the physician treating himself: he effects insofar as he is knowledgeable in the art, and is affected insofar as he is a body that is acted upon by what he encounters of medicine. This is with respect to the apparent usage; otherwise, in reality the effect belongs to the soul and the being-affected to the body. And if he had exemplified by the one treating himself in refinement of morals and change of character traits, it would have been closer. Then the force that is the attribute of effectivity is either with intention and awareness of its effect or not; and in both cases, either its effects are diverse or not. The first—the effective attribute with intention and awareness and diversity of effects and actions—is the animal force called qudra. The second—the effective force by way of intention and awareness but on a single course with no diversity in its effects—is the celestial force. The third—the principle of diverse effects and actions not by way of intention and awareness—is the vegetative force. The fourth—the principle of effect on a single course without intention and awareness—is the elemental force. And all of these are categories of accident as implied by the word "attribute", and they are the proximate principles of actions. But whether each or some of them have principles from the category of substances called specific forms and souls—that is another discussion.

Commentary
This passage defines qudra (capability/power) and classifies forces. It begins by clarifying that the discussion involves differing powers such as celestial and vegetative, and the intended meaning is 'disposition for effect' to include the created capability. Then it gives a standard definition: qudra is an attribute by which the agent is able to perform or leave an action. It is also called the principle of change by intention. The author notes that 'qudra' is used both for animal ability to perform strenuous actions (opposite: weakness) and for the attribute of effectivity (effecting change in another). For the latter, it is defined as an attribute of a thing in another insofar as it is other, requiring distinction between effector and affected, but distinction in consideration (like the physician treating himself) suffices. In reality, the effect belongs to soul and being-affected to body. Then forces are classified by two criteria: presence of intention/awareness, and diversity of effects. Four types emerge: (1) animal force—with intention, awareness, and diverse effects (this is called qudra); (2) celestial force—with intention and awareness but no diversity of effects; (3) vegetative force—without intention/awareness but with diverse effects; (4) elemental force—without intention/awareness and without diversity of effects. All these are accidental attributes (a'rad) and are proximate principles of actions. The passage ends by noting that whether they have substance-level principles (specific forms, souls) is another topic.

Key Terms
قوة (quwwa)
قوة حيوانية (animal force)
قوة فلكية (celestial force)
قوة نباتية (vegetative force)
قوة عنصرية (elemental force)
قصد (intention)
شعور (awareness)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Only animals have intention and awareness in their actions.
Correction: The passage shows that celestial forces also have intention and awareness, though their effects are uniform.

Checkpoint
Which force is characterized by having intention and awareness but only one type of effect (no diversity)?
A) Animal force
B) Celestial force
C) Vegetative force
D) Elemental force

Key Takeaway
Forces are distinguished by whether they involve intention and whether their effects are diverse; this yields four categories: animal, celestial, vegetative, and elemental.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your actions often mirror one of these four forces. Are most of your daily actions done with full intention and varied outcomes (animal force), or do you slip into mechanical repetition (elemental force) without truly engaging your will? Which force dominates your prayer, your work, your relationships?
?
The animal force is called qudra, the same word for Allah's power. Have you ever thanked Allah for giving you a will that can choose between diverse actions, unlike the fixed celestial or blind elemental forces? How does this awareness change how you use your freedom today?
?
The celestial force acts with intention but its effects are uniform — a picture of perfect obedience. In what areas of your life are you still resisting the uniform command of Allah, preferring the chaos of diverse personal whims over the steady orbit of His law?

Two Interpretations of Qudra: Intention vs. Diversity of Effects
Al-Taftazani presents two interpretations of qudra with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two scholarly interpretations of qudra (capability) and how they classify animal, elemental, celestial, and vegetative forces.

Core Idea
Qudra is defined either by intention or by diversity of effects.

In this passage, al-Taftazani builds on the earlier discussion of forces to explain what makes a force a genuine qudra (capability). He presents two competing criteria used by scholars: intention-awareness and diversity of effects. By applying these to animal, elemental, celestial, and vegetative forces, he shows how each interpretation yields a different classification.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to understand the overall argument.
2
Identify the two interpretations
The passage presents two definitions of qudra. Find the key condition for each: intention/awareness for the first, diversity of effects for the second.
Notice that each interpretation excludes certain types of forces.
3
Apply to the four forces
The passage applies the interpretations to animal, elemental, celestial, and vegetative forces. Determine which forces qualify under each interpretation and why.
Focus on the reasoning: presence or absence of intention and diversity.
4
Understand the relationship between the two interpretations
The passage concludes that the two interpretations have a 'general-specific in some respects' relationship. Reflect on how they overlap and differ.
This is a conceptual relationship, not a logical contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إذا تقرر هذا فنقول : اعتبار بعضهم في كون القوة قدرة مقارنتها للقصد والشعور ففسر القدرة بصفة تؤثر وفق الإرادة فخرج من الصفات ما لا يؤثر كالعلم وما يؤثر لا على وفق الإرادة كالقوى النباتية والعنصرية، وأما النفوس والصور النوعية التي هي من قبيل الجواهر فلا تشملها الصفة . واعتبر بعضهم اختلاف الآثار. ففسر القدرة بصفة تكون مبدأ لأفعال مختلفة فالقوة الحيوانية تكون قدرة بالتفسيرين لمقارنتها القصد والاختلاف والقوة العنصرية لا تكون قدرة بشيء من التفسيرين لخلوها عن الأمرين، والقوة الفلكية قدرة بالتفسير (۱) الأول دون الثاني والنباتية بالعكس وهذا ظاهر فبين التفسيرين عموم من وجه
Now that this is established, we say: Some scholars considered [the condition] for a force to be a qudra (capability) the accompaniment of intention and awareness, so they interpreted qudra as an attribute that effects in accordance with will; thus there are excluded from [the category of] attributes what does not effect, like knowledge, and what effects not in accordance with will, like the vegetative and elemental forces. As for souls and specific forms which are from the category of substances, the attribute does not include them. And some scholars considered diversity of effects. So they interpreted qudra as an attribute that is a principle of diverse actions. Thus the animal force is a qudra by both interpretations because it accompanies intention and [produces] diversity; the elemental force is not a qudra by either interpretation because it lacks both [conditions]; the celestial force is a qudra by the first interpretation but not the second, and the vegetative force is the opposite. This is apparent, so between the two interpretations there is a general-specific [relationship] in some respects (عموم من وجه).
Source pages p. 348

Commentary
This passage presents two scholarly interpretations of qudra (capability). First, some scholars stipulate that a force is a qudra only if it is accompanied by intention (qasd) and awareness (shu'ur). According to this interpretation, qudra is an attribute that effects in accordance with will (irada). This excludes attributes that do not effect (like knowledge) and attributes that effect but not in accordance with will (like vegetative and elemental forces). It also excludes souls and specific forms (substances) because the term 'attribute' (sifa) does not apply to them. Second, other scholars stipulate diversity of effects (ikhtilaf al-athar): qudra is a principle of diverse actions. The author then applies these two interpretations to the four types of forces from the previous lesson. The animal force qualifies as qudra by both interpretations because it involves both intention and diversity. The elemental force qualifies by neither because it lacks both. The celestial force qualifies only by the first (it has intention but no diversity). The vegetative force qualifies only by the second (it has diversity but no intention). The relationship between the two interpretations is 'general-specific in some respects' (umum min wajh): they overlap partially—some forces satisfy both, some only one, some neither.

Key Terms
قدرة (qudra/capability)
قصد (intention)
شعور (awareness)
إرادة (will)
اختلاف الآثار (diversity of effects)
عموم من وجه (general-specific in some respects)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the two interpretations are equivalent or that only one is correct.
Correction: The passage shows they are different and complementary, each highlighting a necessary aspect of qudra.

Checkpoint
Which force is considered a qudra by the first interpretation (requiring intention/awareness) but not by the second (requiring diversity of effects)?
A) Animal force
B) Celestial force
C) Vegetative force
D) Elemental force

Key Takeaway
Qudra is defined either by intention-awareness or by diversity of effects; these interpretations are complementary and not contradictory.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have been created with a qudra that requires intention and awareness. Every action you perform with deliberate will is a sign of this gift. But how often do you reflect on the fact that your very ability to intend and choose is a created attribute from Allah? Imagine a day when you lost all intention—your movements robotic. Would you still be you? This lesson forces you to see that your qudra is not self-sufficient; it is a trust from the One who alone has absolute qudra. How will you use your intentional acts today to acknowledge that trust?
?
The animal force is qudra by both interpretations—it has intention and produces diverse effects. Yet humans are distinguished by higher qudra: the ability to reason and choose for akhirah. Look at your own life: do your intentional actions produce diverse effects that serve only this world, or do they also produce effects for the next? The angel has intention but uniform effects—constant worship. Your qudra allows you to vary your actions. Are you using that diversity to draw closer to Allah, or are you wasting it on worldly diversity?
?
The vegetative force has no intention but produces diverse effects. It is qudra only by the second interpretation. You are not like a plant; you have intention. But do you sometimes act like a plant—going through motions without awareness? This lesson warns that qudra without intention is not true qudra in the full sense. In your acts of worship, is your qudra accompanied by qasd and shu'ur, or have you become a creature of habit, producing effects but lacking the inner intention that makes them count?

Definition of Fi'l: Lexical, Grammatical, and Philosophical
The author defines fi'l lexically, grammatically, and philosophically.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three distinct definitions of the term fi'l (action) in Arabic: lexical, grammatical, and philosophical.

Core Idea
Fi'l means different things in language, grammar, and philosophy.

The term fi'l (action) is used across multiple disciplines with subtle differences in meaning. In this passage, the author clarifies the lexical, grammatical, and philosophical senses, providing examples to distinguish them. Understanding these distinctions is essential for reading classical texts in Arabic grammar and philosophy.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Note the different contexts in which fi'l is defined.
Pay attention to the examples given for each definition.
2
Identify the three definitions
Locate the lexical definition (first sentence), the grammatical definition (second sentence), and the philosophical definition (after 'وفي اصطلاح الفلاسفة').
The philosophical definition has two sub-senses: general sense (natural and artificial actions) and human actions.
3
Compare lexical and philosophical definitions
Notice that the lexical definition focuses on the accidental form (hay'a) that arises from being an agent, while the philosophical general sense emphasizes being an agent affecting another.
The example of the cutter appears in both lexical and philosophical sections.
4
Test your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question to ensure you can apply the philosophical definition correctly.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفعل : هو العمل، والهيئة العارضة للمؤثر في غيره بسبب التأثير أولا كالهيئة الحاصلة للقاطع بسبب كونه قاطعاً، وفي اصطلاح النحاة ما دل على معنى في نفسه مقترن بأحد الأزمنة الثلاثة . راجع تعريفات الجرجاني . وللفعل في اصطلاح الفلاسفة عدة معان . فالفعل بالمعنى العام يطلق على كونه الشيء مؤثراً في غيره ومثاله : أفعال الطبيعة كتأثير النار في التسخين فهي فاعلة والمتسخن منفعل. وأفعال الصناعة كالقاطع ما دام قاطعاً . ويطلق الفعل أيضاً على ما يقوم به الانسان من أفعال إرادية أو غير إرادية .
Al-Fi'l (action): it is the work, and the accidental form (al-hay'a) that occurs to the agent affecting something else due to the effect initially, like the form obtained for a cutter due to its being a cutter. In the terminology of grammarians, it is what indicates a meaning in itself associated with one of the three tenses. See al-Jurjani's definitions. In the terminology of philosophers, al-fi'l has several meanings. In the general sense, it is predicated of a thing being an agent affecting something else, e.g., natural actions like the effect of fire in heating; so it is an agent, and the heated is acted upon. And artificial actions, like the cutter as long as it is cutting. Also, al-fi'l is predicated of what proceeds from a human being of voluntary or involuntary actions.
Source pages p. 352

Commentary
This passage provides a lexical and technical definition of the term fi'l (فعل). The author first gives a general definition: it is 'work' (العمل), and more precisely an accidental form (هيئة عارضة) that arises in an agent due to its affecting something else, like the 'cutter' (قاطع) acquiring the form of cutting. Then the grammatical definition familiar from Arabic grammar: a word that indicates a meaning in itself associated with one of the three tenses (past, present, future). Finally, the philosophical definition: in a general sense, it means the thing's being an agent affecting another, with examples from natural actions (fire heating) and artificial actions (a cutting tool). It also includes human voluntary and involuntary actions.

هيئة
صناعة
طبيعة
نحو

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some students think that the grammatical definition of fi'l (verb) is the same as the philosophical sense of 'action'.
Correction: The text shows clear separation: grammatically fi'l is a part of speech tied to tense, while philosophically it refers to being an agent or an action itself. They are distinct technical terms.

Key Takeaway
Fi'l has distinct meanings in lexicography, grammar, and philosophy; the general philosophical sense refers to an agent affecting another.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The lexical definition calls fi'l an 'accidental form' that arises only when an agent affects something. Definition of Khuluq and Its Opposition to Qudra
Defines khuluq as a psychological quality and explains its opposition to qudra.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of khuluq (moral disposition) as a deeply rooted faculty from which actions issue easily without deliberation and with inclination, and why it is opposed to qudra (ability).

Core Idea
Khuluq makes actions flow easily with inclination; qudra is neutral and equal toward opposites.

In this passage, the author defines khuluq (disposition/character) and explains why it is considered contrary to qudra (ability). Understanding this distinction is essential for grasping how actions are attributed to agents in kalam. Let's read the source text and commentary to see the precise conditions that make something a khuluq and why qudra falls outside that definition.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then read the literal translation. Let the author's own description of khuluq and its opposition sink in.
The passage begins with 'ويضادها الخلق' – note the immediate contrast.
2
Identify the definition of khuluq
Find the statement that defines khuluq as a 'malaka' (faculty) with specific characteristics: actions issue easily, without deliberation, and its relation to the two extremes (action and omission) is not equal.
This definition is the core. Compare it with qudra described later.
3
List the exclusions
The passage explains what is NOT khuluq: non-rooted states (e.g., occasional anger), rooted faculties that relate equally to opposites (like qudra), and those requiring thought (e.g., miser trying to be generous). Identify each example.
These exclusions clarify the boundaries of the definition.
4
Understand why qudra is opposed
The passage concludes that qudra opposes khuluq because qudra lacks ease, requires no deliberation, and relates equally to both action and omission. Reflect on this contrast.
This opposition is a well-known distinction in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : ويضادها الخلق من جهة أنه ملكة تصدر بها الأفعال عن النفس بسهولة، ومن غير روية، وان نسبته إلى الطرفين لا تكون على السوية . يريد أن من الكيفيات النفسانية الخلق، وفسر بملكة تصدر بها عن النفس أفعال بسهولة من غير تقدم فكر وروية فغير الراسخ من صفات النفس لا يكون خلقاً كغضب الحليم، وكذا الراسخ الذي يكون مبدأ لأفعال الجوارح بسهولة كملكة الكتابة أو يكون نسبته إلى الفعل والترك على السواء كالقدرة، أو يفتقر في صدور الفعل عنه إلى فكر وروية كالبخيل إذا حاول الكرم، وكالكريم إذا قصد بالعطاء الشهرة، ولكا كانت القدرة تصدر عنها الفعل لا بسهولة واستغناء عن روية وكانت نسبتها إلى طرفي الفعل والترك على السوية حكم بأنها تضاد الخلق مضادة مشهورة .
He said: And it (ability) is opposed by disposition (al-khuluq) in that it is a faculty (malaka) through which actions issue from the soul easily and without deliberation, and its relation to the two extremes is not equal. He means that among the psychological qualities (al-kayfiyyāt al-nafsāniyya) is disposition, and it is interpreted as a faculty through which actions issue from the soul easily without preceding thought and deliberation. So what is not deeply rooted (rāsikh) among the attributes of the soul is not a disposition, like the anger of a forbearing person. Likewise, the deeply rooted that is a principle for the actions of the limbs easily, like the faculty of writing, or whose relation to action and abandonment is equal, like ability, or which requires thought and deliberation for the action to issue, like the miser when he attempts generosity, and like the generous when he intends fame by giving. When ability (qudra) causes the action to issue without ease and independence from deliberation, and its relation to the two extremes of action and abandonment is equal, it is judged that it opposes disposition in a well-known opposition.
Source pages p. 363

Commentary
This passage defines the concept of khuluq (moral disposition/character) and explains why it is considered contrary to qudra (ability). The author states that khuluq is a malaka (firmly rooted quality) from which actions proceed easily and without deliberation, and crucially, its inclination towards action or omission is not balanced—it predisposes one to one side. The author elaborates: non-steady attributes (like occasional anger) are not khuluq. Even steady attributes that give rise to actions easily (like writing skill) or that relate equally to both action and omission (like qudra) are not khuluq. Also, attributes that require thought (like the miser trying to be generous) are not khuluq. Since qudra issues actions without ease and relates equally to opposites, it is opposed to khuluq. This is a standard distinction in kalam between inborn capacities (qudra) and acquired dispositions (khuluq).

Key Terms
خلق
ملكة
قدرة
مضادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that any source of action, like ability (qudra), is the same as disposition (khuluq) because both produce actions.
Correction: The passage clarifies that khuluq is a deeply rooted quality that produces actions easily with inclination, while qudra produces actions without ease and relates equally to action and omission, making them opposites.

Checkpoint
What is the defining characteristic of khuluq (disposition) that distinguishes it from qudra (ability)?
A) It is acquired by learning.
B) It issues actions easily and its relation to the two extremes is not equal.
C) It requires deliberation.
D) It is a temporary state.

Key Takeaway
Khuluq is a deeply rooted faculty that produces actions easily and with inclination, making it opposed to the neutral and equal relation of qudra.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform a good deed, does it come easily and naturally (indicating a developed khuluq), or do you feel internal resistance and need to force yourself? What does that tell you about your current character and what you need to work on?
?
Are there any actions that are so deeply rooted in you that they flow without thought—whether positive (like patience) or negative (like anger)? How can you intentionally reshape those ingrained patterns to align with what pleases Allah?
?
Your ability (qudra) is neutral towards good and bad actions, yet your soul inclines towards some more than others. Given that khuluq is not fixed, what concrete steps will you take today to train your soul so that righteous actions become your natural disposition?

Pleasure and Pain: Self-Evident Perceptions
Defines pleasure and pain, discusses their interpretation as perceptions of the suitable and unsuitable, and presents Ibn Sina's definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definitions of pleasure and pain as psychological qualities, the debate over their relationship to perception, and why Ibn Sina's definition is considered the closest.

Core Idea
Pleasure and pain are not mere perceptions but involve attaining what is suitable or harmful.

This passage from Sharh al-Maqasid addresses the nature of pleasure and pain. Although they are self-evident experiences, scholars have debated their precise definition. The author surveys various interpretations, refutes one alternative, and settles on Ibn Sina's nuanced view that pleasure involves both perception and attainment of a perfection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Let the author's own flow come through before analyzing.
Notice the self-evident claim at the beginning.
2
Identify the basic definition and its nuance
Find where pleasure is defined as perception of the suitable. Note the caveat about 'insofar as' and the condition that perception must reach the essence, not just the image.
The example of the jaundiced person shows the importance of the aspect.
3
Understand the refutation of an alternative definition
The author rejects the idea that pleasure is simply an exit from an unnatural state, using examples of pleasure from wealth or beauty without prior desire.
This refutation narrows the possible definitions.
4
Grasp Ibn Sina's refinement
Ibn Sina adds 'attainment' (nayl) to perception, because mere awareness of something suitable is not enough; one must actually receive the perfection.
This definition becomes the preferred one in the text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: ومنها اللذة والألم وهما بديهيان، وقد يفسران بإدراك الملائم والمنافي من حيث هما كذلك فهما نوعان من الإدراك اعتبر فيهما إضافة تختلف بالقياس إلى المدرك وإضافة لذات الملائم أو المنافي لا صورتهما والحق أنا نجد حالة هي اللذة، ونعلم أن ثمة إدراكاً للملائم واما انهما نفسه، أو أمر حاصل به . وهل يحصل بغيره، ففيه تردد. وقد يفسر بالخروج عن الحالة الغير الطبيعية ويبطله الالتذاذ بإصابة مال، أو مطالعة جمال من غير طلب وشوق . أي من الكيفيات النفسانية اللذة والألم وتصورهما بديهي كسائر الوجدانيات، وقد يفسران قصداً إلى تعيين المسمى وتلخيصه فيقال: اللذة إدراك الملائم من حيث هو ملائم، والألم إدراك المنافي من حيث هو مناف والملائم للشيء كماله الخاص أعني الأمر اللائق بـه گالتكيف بالحلاوة للذائقة، وتعقل الأشياء على ما هي عليه للعاقلة، وقيد بالحيثية لأن الشيء قد يكون ملائماً من وجه دون وجه فإدراكه لا من جهة الملائمة لا يكون لذة كالصفراوي لا يلتذ بالحلو، والمراد بالإدراك الوصول إلى ذات الملائم لا إلى مجرد صورته، فان تخيل اللذيذ غير اللذة ولذا كان الأقرب ما قال ابن سينا : إن اللذة إدراك ونيل لوصول ما هو عند المدرك كمال وخير من حيث هو كذلك، والألم إدراك ونيل لوصول ما هو عند المدرك آفة وشر من حيث هو كذلك، فذكر مع الإدراك النيل. أعني الإصابة والوجدان، لأن إدراك وقد قيل: إن اللذة إدراك الملائم من حيث إنه ملائم كطعم الحلاوة عند حاسة الذوق، والنور عند البصر.
He said: And among them (the psychological qualities) are pleasure (al-ladhdha) and pain (al-alam), and they are self-evident (badīhiyyān). They may be interpreted as perception (idrāk) of the suitable (mulā’im) and the unsuitable (munāfī) insofar as they are such. So they are two types of perception in which an addition (iḍāfa) is considered that differs relative to the perceiver, and an addition to the essences of the suitable or unsuitable, not their forms. The truth is that we find a state that is pleasure, and we know that there is a perception of the suitable; but whether they are the same thing or something obtained by it [is debated]. And whether it can be obtained by something else is subject to hesitation. It may be interpreted as exit from the unnatural state, but this is refuted by the pleasure of obtaining wealth or viewing beauty without seeking or desire. That is, among the psychological qualities are pleasure and pain, and their conception is self-evident like other found things (wijdāniyyāt). They are interpreted intentionally to specify the referent and summarize it, so it is said: Pleasure is the perception of the suitable insofar as it is suitable, and pain is the perception of the unsuitable insofar as it is unsuitable. The suitable for a thing is its particular perfection (kamāl), meaning the thing proper to it, like the qualification with sweetness for the taste, and the intellection of things as they are for the intellect. The condition of 'insofar as' is because a thing may be suitable from one aspect and not from another, so perceiving it not from the aspect of suitability is not pleasure, like the jaundiced person does not take pleasure in sweet. What is meant by perception is reaching the essence of the suitable, not merely its image, for imagining the pleasurable is not pleasure. Hence what is closest is what Ibn Sina said: Pleasure is perception and attainment (nayl) of the arrival of what is for the perceiver a perfection and good insofar as it is such, and pain is perception and attainment of the arrival of what is for the perceiver a harm and evil insofar as it is such. So he mentioned attainment along with perception, meaning reaching and finding, because perception... And it has been said: Pleasure is perception of the suitable insofar as it is suitable, like the taste of sweetness for the sense of taste, and light for sight.
Source pages p. 363

Commentary
This passage discusses the psychological qualities of pleasure and pain. The author begins by stating that they are self-evident (badīhi), meaning they are directly known through experience. They are often defined as the perception (idrāk) of what is suitable (mulā’im) or unsuitable (munāfī) to a being. However, the truth is that we find a state called pleasure and we know there is perception of the suitable, but it is debated whether pleasure is identical to that perception or something caused by it, and whether it can be produced by something else. The author refutes a proposed definition (pleasure as exit from an unnatural state) by counterexample: one can take pleasure in acquiring wealth or viewing beauty without prior desire. The standard definition is then given: pleasure is perception of the suitable as suitable, and pain is perception of the unsuitable as unsuitable, with 'suitable' defined as the perfection proper to a thing (e.g., sweetness for taste, true understanding for intellect). The qualifier 'insofar as' is important because something can be suitable in one respect and unsuitable in another—perceiving it from the unsuitable aspect does not produce pleasure (e.g., a jaundiced person does not enjoy sweetness). Moreover, 'perception' here means reaching the essence of the suitable, not merely its mental image, because imagining something pleasurable is not the same as experiencing pleasure. The author concludes that Ibn Sina's definition is the closest: pleasure and pain involve both perception and attainment (nayl) of the arrival of a perfection or harm, because mere perception without actual attainment would not suffice. A simpler definition is also given: pleasure is the perception of the suitable, like sweetness for taste or light for sight.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Pleasure is simply the perception of something suitable.
Correction: According to the passage, pleasure requires both perception and actual attainment (nayl) of the suitable perfection, not just its mental image.

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the closest definition of pleasure?
A) The mere perception of the suitable.
B) The exit from an unnatural state.
C) Perception and attainment of what is a perfection for the perceiver.
D) A self-evident state that cannot be defined.

Key Takeaway
Pleasure and pain are self-evident but require a nuanced definition that includes both perception and actual attainment of what is suitable or harmful.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on your daily pursuits, do you often mistake the mere image or anticipation of pleasure (e.g., scrolling through social media) for the actual attainment of something genuinely suitable for your soul? What would change if you began to differentiate between the two?
?
The text defines pleasure as attaining a perfection that is truly good for you. What is one area of your life—your intellect, character, or worship—where you have settled for the perception of progress without the reality of attainment? What step can you take today to actually reach that perfection?
?
Ibn Sina insists that pleasure requires not just perception but actual arrival of the good. Think of a time you experienced deep pleasure—was it from merely thinking about something or from genuinely receiving it? How can you apply this insight to your relationship with the Hereafter, where the ultimate pleasure is vision of Allah? Are you longing for the image or the reality?

Pleasure as Perception of the Agreeable
Taftazani defines pleasure and pain and explains their relativity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that pleasure and pain are types of perception relative to the agreeable or disagreeable, and how they depend on the perceiver's belief rather than objective reality.

Core Idea
Pleasure is perception of the agreeable, not the thing itself.

Taftazani begins by clarifying what pleasure and pain are. He argues that pleasure is not merely the presence of a pleasant object but the perception of its attainment. He also distinguishes between perfection and good, showing that what we consider perfect or good is relative to the perceiver. This lesson covers the definition, the distinction, and the objection about the patient.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of pleasure
Read the first lines stating that pleasure is completed by the occurrence of the essence, not an image. Note the distinction between attaining something and attaining its image.
Pleasure requires actual attainment, not just representation.
2
Understand the distinction between perfection and good
Perfection is the acquisition of what is suitable for a thing, bringing it from potentiality to actuality. Good is that acquisition considered as effective. Both are relative to the perceiver.
Something can be perfect without being perceived as good.
3
Relativity to the perceiver
Notice that pleasure depends on belief in perfection/goodness. Different people may have different pleasures from the same thing based on their beliefs.
This explains why tastes vary.
4
The objection and its answer
Read the objection about the patient liking harmful sweetness. See how the 'insofar as' (ḥaythiyya) clause resolves it: pleasure is in the perception of agreeableness, not in actual benefit.
Pleasure is about perceived agreeableness, not objective benefit.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشيء قد يكون بحصول صورة تساويه، ونيله لا يكون إلا بحصول ذاته، واللذة لا تتم بحصول ما يساوي اللذيذ، بل إنما يتم بحصول ذاته. وذكر إدراك حصول اللذيذ للملتذ، ووصوله إليه والفرق بين الكمال (۱) والخير هو أن حصول ما يناسب الشيء ويليق به من حيث اقتضائه براءة ما لذلك الشيء من القوة إلى الفعل كمال له ومن حيث كونه مؤثراً خير ، ثم المعتبر كماليته وخيريته بالقياس إلى المدرك لا في نفس الأمر لأنه قد يعتقد الكمالية والخيرية في شيء فيستلذ به، وإن لم يكونا فيه وقد لا يعتقدهما فيما تحققنا فيه فلا يلتذ به، ولهذا يحصل من شيء معين لذة، أو ألم لزيد دون عمرو وبالعكس، فكل من اللذة والألم نوع من الإدراك اعتبر فيه إضافة إلى ملائم، أو مناف يختلف بالقياس إلى المدرك، وإصابة ووجدان لذات الملائم، أو المنافي من حيث هو كذلك لا للصورة الحاصلة منه، وبقيد الحيثية يندفع ما يقال : إن المريض قد يلتذ بالحلاوة مع أنها لا تلائمه (۲) بل تضره وينفر عن الأدوية، وهي وتنفعه، وذكر الإمام بعد الاعتراف بأن اللذة والألم حقيقتان غنيتان عن التعريف أنا نجد من أنفسنا حالة نسميها باللذة ونعرف أن هناك إدراكاً للملائم لكن لم يثبت لنا أن اللذة نفس إدراك الملائم أو غيره، وبتقدير المغايرة هل هي معلولة له أم لا؟ وبتقدير المعلولية هل يمكن حصولها بطريق آخر؟
A thing may be [attained] by the occurrence of an image equal to it, but its attainment is only by the occurrence of its essence. Pleasure is not completed by the occurrence of what equals the pleasant thing, but rather is completed by the occurrence of its essence. And he mentioned the perception of the occurrence of the pleasant thing for the one experiencing pleasure, and its reaching him, and the difference between perfection (al-kamāl) and good (al-khayr) is that the occurrence of what is suitable and fitting for a thing, insofar as it necessitates its emergence from potentiality to actuality, is perfection for it, and insofar as it is effective, it is good. Then what is considered of its perfection and goodness is relative to the perceiver, not in itself, because one may believe perfection and goodness in a thing and thus take pleasure in it, even if they are not in it, and one may not believe them in something in which they are realized and thus not take pleasure in it. Hence a particular thing may cause pleasure or pain for Zayd but not for Amr, and vice versa. So both pleasure and pain are a type of perception in which an addition to the agreeable or the disagreeable is considered, differing relative to the perceiver. And they are an attainment and finding of pleasures of the agreeable or the disagreeable insofar as it is so, not for the image obtained from it. And by the restriction of 'insofar as' (al-ḥaythiyya) what is said is repelled: that the patient may take pleasure in sweetness even though it is not agreeable to him but harms him, and he dislikes medicines even though they benefit him. And the Imām mentioned, after acknowledging that pleasure and pain are two realities independent of definition, that we find in ourselves a state we call pleasure and we know that there is a perception of the agreeable, but it has not been proven to us that pleasure is the same as the perception of the agreeable or something else. And assuming difference, is it caused by it or not? And assuming causality, can it occur by another way?
Source pages p. 365

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and explanatory discussion of the nature of pleasure and pain. It begins by stating that pleasure is not merely the occurrence of an image equal to the pleasant thing, but requires the occurrence of the essence itself. Then it distinguishes between perfection (kamāl) and good (khayr): perfection is the acquisition of what is fitting for a thing, bringing it from potentiality to actuality; good is considered when that acquisition is effective. However, the attribution of perfection and goodness is relative to the perceiver, as one may take pleasure in something believed to be perfect even if it is not, or fail to take pleasure in something truly perfect due to lack of belief. Thus pleasure and pain are both types of perception that involve an addition to the agreeable or disagreeable, and they vary among individuals. The passage then defends this view against an objection: a patient may take pleasure in sweetness even though it harms him, but this is explained by the 'insofar as' clause — pleasure is in the perception of the agreeable as such, not in the actual benefit. Finally, the Imām (likely al-Rāzī) notes that although pleasure and pain are self-evident, it remains uncertain whether pleasure is identical to perception of the agreeable or something separate, and if separate, whether it is caused by that perception and whether it can occur otherwise.

الحيثية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Pleasure is simply the perception of the agreeable without qualification.
Correction: The passage explains that pleasure is the perception of the agreeable insofar as it is agreeable, not for the image obtained. This 'insofar as' qualification allows for cases like the patient taking pleasure in harmful sweetness.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does a patient take pleasure in sweetness that harms him?
A) Because pleasure is based on belief in perfection, not actual benefit.
B) Because pleasure is the perception of the agreeable insofar as it is agreeable, not the image of benefit.
C) Because the patient's temperament is altered.
D) Because pleasure is independent of perception.

Key Takeaway
Pleasure and pain are perceptions relative to the perceiver's belief in agreeableness, not objective benefit.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time when you took pleasure in something you later realized was harmful. How did the 'insofar as' qualifier operate in that moment—were you perceiving the agreeableness without considering the actual harm? What would it mean for you to align your perception of pleasure with true benefit, not just belief?
?
The passage says perfection and goodness are relative to the perceiver. Are you pursuing goals that you believe are perfect and good but may not actually be? What would it take for you to honestly examine whether your pursuits are bringing you closer to actual perfection or just perceived pleasure?
?
If pleasure is perception of the agreeable, and that perception can be mistaken, how do you guard against being deceived by false pleasures? This lesson challenges you to interrogate your assumptions about what is truly agreeable to your soul. What is one false pleasure you are currently holding onto, and what step can you take today to realign your perception?

The Imam's Questions on Pleasure and Pain
Taftazani reports al-Rāzī's analysis of the uncertainty surrounding pleasure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the exact relationship between pleasure and perception of the agreeable remains an open question in kalam.

Core Idea
Pleasure is known by experience, but its identity with perception is not proven.

After establishing that pleasure and pain are perceptions of the agreeable or disagreeable, Taftazani presents the Imām's (al-Rāzī's) reservations. Although we know pleasure directly, we cannot be certain whether it is identical to the perception of the agreeable or something distinct, and whether it is caused by that perception. This lesson examines those open questions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage acknowledging pleasure as self-evident
The Imam admits that pleasure and pain are realities that need no definition; we directly experience them.
2
Note the three questions raised
The Imam raises three questions: (1) Is pleasure the same as perception of the agreeable? (2) If different, is it caused by that perception? (3) If caused, can it occur through another way?
These are open inquiries, not settled claims.
3
Reflect on the significance
These questions show that kalam does not take the nature of pleasure for granted; even basic experiences are subject to philosophical analysis.
This humility is important for theological method.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Pain and the Transition Theory of Pleasure
Taftazani clarifies pain and presents the transition theory.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the preliminary claim that pain is not mere perception and Muhammad ibn Zakariyya's definition of pleasure as transition from an unnatural to a natural state.

Core Idea
Pleasure is not always relief from pain; the definition is challenged.

In this passage, Taftazani first clarifies that pain is not identical to perceiving something disagreeable, supported by medical evidence. He then presents the influential theory of Muhammad ibn Zakariyya (al-Rāzī) that pleasure is the change from an unnatural to a natural state, citing Galen. This sets the stage for a refutation by Ibn Sīnā in the next lesson.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening statement
Read the first sentence where Taftazani states that pain is not simply the perception of the disagreeable, nor is that perception sufficient for pain. Note the medical evidence about moist bad temperament.
This is a preliminary clarification before discussing pleasure.
2
Identify the theory of pleasure
Find the part where Muhammad ibn Zakariyya defines pleasure as change from an unnatural to a natural state. Notice that Galen agrees, and that this is equated with relief from pain.
Examples like eating when hungry illustrate the theory.
3
Reflect on the examples
Consider the examples given: eating for hunger, intercourse for the tickling of vessels. How do they support the theory that pleasure is a return to a natural state?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم قال : والأقرب أن الألم ليس هو نفس إدراك المنافي ولا هو كاف في حصوله، لأن التجارب الطبية قد شهدت له (۱) بأن سوء المزاج الرطب غير مؤلم مع أن هناك إدراك أمر غير طبيعي، وسنتكلم على ذلك، وزعم محمد ابن زكريا أن اللذة عبارة عن التبدل والخروج عن حالة غير طبيعية (٢) إلى حالة طبيعية، وبه صرح جالينوس (۳) في مواضع من كلامه، وهو معنى الخلاص عن الألم، وذلك كالأكل للجوع ، والجماع لدغدغة المنى أوعيته . وأبطله ابن سينا وغيره بأنه قد تحصل اللذة من غير سابقة ألم، وحالة غير طبيعية كما في مصادفة حال ومطالعة جمال من غير طلب وشوق لا على التفصيل ولا على الإجمال بأن لم يخطر ذلك بباله قط (٤) لا جزئياً ولا كلياً، وكذا في إدراك الذائقة الحلاوة أول مرة، وقد يحصل ذلك التبدل من غير لذة كما في حصول الصحة على التدريج وفي ورود المستلذات من الطعوم والروائح والأصوات وغيرها على من له غاية الشوق إلى ذلك، وقد عرض لـه شاغل عن الشعور والإدراك قالوا : وسبب السهو أخذ ما بالعرض مكان ما بالذات، فان الألم واللذة لا يتمان إلا بإدراك، والإدراك الحسي خصوصاً اللمسي لا يحصل إلا بانفعال عن العند (٥) ، ولذلك متى استقرت الكيفية الموجبة لذلك لم يحصل الانفعال (٦) فلم يحصل الإدراك بل تحصل لذة ولا ألم .
Then he said: The closer [opinion] is that pain is not itself the perception of the disagreeable, nor is it sufficient for its occurrence, because medical experiences testify that a moist bad temperament is painless even though there is perception of something unnatural, and we will discuss that. And Muhammad ibn Zakariyya claimed that pleasure is the change and exit from an unnatural state to a natural state, and Galen stated this in places in his speech, and it is the meaning of relief from pain, such as eating for hunger and intercourse for the tickling of the vessels of semen. But Ibn Sīnā and others refuted it because pleasure can occur without a preceding pain and unnatural state, such as encountering a situation and viewing beauty without request or longing, neither in detail nor in general, in that it never crossed his mind at all, neither partially nor wholly. Likewise in the perception of sweetness by the taste bud for the first time. And that change may occur without pleasure, as in the gradual attainment of health and in the arrival of pleasurable things of tastes, smells, sounds, etc., to one who has extreme longing for that but is preoccupied from feeling and perception. They said: The cause of the oversight is taking the accidental in place of the essential. For pain and pleasure are not completed except by perception, and sensory perception, especially tactile, does not occur except by being affected (infiʿāl) from the opposite. Therefore, when the quality causing that becomes stable, the being affected does not occur, so perception does not occur, and neither pleasure nor pain occurs.
Source pages p. 366

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer argument. It begins by stating the closer opinion that pain is not identical to the perception of the disagreeable, nor is that perception sufficient for pain, as medical experiments show that a moist bad temperament involves perception of something unnatural but is painless. Then it presents the theory of Muhammad ibn Zakariyya (likely al-Rāzī) that pleasure is the change from an unnatural state to a natural state, citing Galen, and that this is equivalent to relief from pain (e.g., eating when hungry). The refutation by Ibn Sīnā and others follows: pleasure can occur without preceding pain or unnatural state, such as first-time tasting of sweetness or seeing beauty without prior thought. Conversely, the change can occur without pleasure, as when health returns gradually or when one is distracted from pleasurable sensations. The refutation concludes by diagnosing the error: taking the accidental (ʿaraḍ) for the essential (dhāt). Pain and pleasure require perception, and sensory perception, especially tactile, requires being affected (infiʿāl) by an opposite; when the quality stabilizes, no affection occurs, hence no perception, and thus no pleasure or pain.

Key Terms
اللذة
الألم
التبدل
الحالة الطبيعية
محمد بن زكريا
جالينوس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All pleasure arises from relief of pain or return to a natural state.
Correction: The passage presents this as a theory, not a settled fact, and the next lesson will show counterexamples.

Checkpoint
According to Muhammad ibn Zakariyya, what is pleasure?
A) Perception of the agreeable
B) Change from an unnatural to a natural state
C) Relief from pain
D) Both B and C

Key Takeaway
Pleasure is not merely relief from pain; the definition as transition from unnatural to natural is one theory that requires scrutiny.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience deep pleasure, do you automatically think it came from a previous pain or discomfort? How does this lesson challenge you to reconsider that assumption in your spiritual life—are you seeking pleasure only as escape from hardship, or do you recognize pleasures that come purely from goodness?
?
The passage shows that pain is not simply perceiving something unpleasant. In your own life, can you identify moments when you felt a disagreeable sensation but were not in pain? How can this distinction help you respond to discomfort with patience rather than panic?
?
If pleasure is not merely relief from pain, then what are you truly seeking when you pursue enjoyment? This lesson calls you to look beyond the surface—will you examine your desires today to see if they aim at a genuine natural state or just a momentary distraction?
