Ibn Sīnā's Refutation of the Transition Theory
Ibn Sīnā refutes the transition theory with counterexamples and error analysis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn Sīnā's counterexamples to the transition theory and the diagnosis of the error as mistaking the accidental for the essential.

Core Idea
Pleasure can occur without prior pain, and change can occur without pleasure.

This passage presents the refutation of Muhammad ibn Zakariyya's theory by Ibn Sīnā and others. It provides clear counterexamples: pleasure without preceding pain (e.g., first-time tasting sweetness) and change without pleasure (e.g., gradual health). The refutation concludes by identifying the root cause: taking the accidental for the essential.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation passage
Read from 'وأبطله ابن سينا وغيره' to the end. Identify the two main points: pleasure without prior pain, and change without pleasure.
The refutation uses concrete examples.
2
Analyze the first counterexample
Find the examples where pleasure occurs without a preceding unnatural state: encountering beauty without prior thought and tasting sweetness for the first time. Notice that there is no prior pain or longing.
These show that pleasure can be spontaneous.
3
Analyze the second counterexample
Find the examples where the change from unnatural to natural state occurs without pleasure: gradual health recovery and arrival of desired things when distracted. Note that change happens but no pleasure because perception is absent.
This shows that change alone is insufficient for pleasure.
4
Understand the cause of error
Read the final part where the error is diagnosed: taking the accidental (the change) for the essential (perception). Pain and pleasure require perception, which requires being affected, and when the quality stabilizes, no affection occurs.
This is a key analytical insight.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم قال : والأقرب أن الألم ليس هو نفس إدراك المنافي ولا هو كاف في حصوله، لأن التجارب الطبية قد شهدت له (۱) بأن سوء المزاج الرطب غير مؤلم مع أن هناك إدراك أمر غير طبيعي، وسنتكلم على ذلك، وزعم محمد ابن زكريا أن اللذة عبارة عن التبدل والخروج عن حالة غير طبيعية (٢) إلى حالة طبيعية، وبه صرح جالينوس (۳) في مواضع من كلامه، وهو معنى الخلاص عن الألم، وذلك كالأكل للجوع ، والجماع لدغدغة المنى أوعيته . وأبطله ابن سينا وغيره بأنه قد تحصل اللذة من غير سابقة ألم، وحالة غير طبيعية كما في مصادفة حال ومطالعة جمال من غير طلب وشوق لا على التفصيل ولا على الإجمال بأن لم يخطر ذلك بباله قط (٤) لا جزئياً ولا كلياً، وكذا في إدراك الذائقة الحلاوة أول مرة، وقد يحصل ذلك التبدل من غير لذة كما في حصول الصحة على التدريج وفي ورود المستلذات من الطعوم والروائح والأصوات وغيرها على من له غاية الشوق إلى ذلك، وقد عرض لـه شاغل عن الشعور والإدراك قالوا : وسبب السهو أخذ ما بالعرض مكان ما بالذات، فان الألم واللذة لا يتمان إلا بإدراك، والإدراك الحسي خصوصاً اللمسي لا يحصل إلا بانفعال عن العند (٥) ، ولذلك متى استقرت الكيفية الموجبة لذلك لم يحصل الانفعال (٦) فلم يحصل الإدراك بل تحصل لذة ولا ألم .
Then he said: The closer [opinion] is that pain is not itself the perception of the disagreeable, nor is it sufficient for its occurrence, because medical experiences testify that a moist bad temperament is painless even though there is perception of something unnatural, and we will discuss that. And Muhammad ibn Zakariyya claimed that pleasure is the change and exit from an unnatural state to a natural state, and Galen stated this in places in his speech, and it is the meaning of relief from pain, such as eating for hunger and intercourse for the tickling of the vessels of semen. But Ibn Sīnā and others refuted it because pleasure can occur without a preceding pain and unnatural state, such as encountering a situation and viewing beauty without request or longing, neither in detail nor in general, in that it never crossed his mind at all, neither partially nor wholly. Likewise in the perception of sweetness by the taste bud for the first time. And that change may occur without pleasure, as in the gradual attainment of health and in the arrival of pleasurable things of tastes, smells, sounds, etc., to one who has extreme longing for that but is preoccupied from feeling and perception. They said: The cause of the oversight is taking the accidental in place of the essential. For pain and pleasure are not completed except by perception, and sensory perception, especially tactile, does not occur except by being affected (infiʿāl) from the opposite. Therefore, when the quality causing that becomes stable, the being affected does not occur, so perception does not occur, and neither pleasure nor pain occurs.
Source pages p. 366

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer argument. It begins by stating the closer opinion that pain is not identical to the perception of the disagreeable, nor is that perception sufficient for pain, as medical experiments show that a moist bad temperament involves perception of something unnatural but is painless. Then it presents the theory of Muhammad ibn Zakariyya (likely al-Rāzī) that pleasure is the change from an unnatural state to a natural state, citing Galen, and that this is equivalent to relief from pain (e.g., eating when hungry). The refutation by Ibn Sīnā and others follows: pleasure can occur without preceding pain or unnatural state, such as first-time tasting of sweetness or seeing beauty without prior thought. Conversely, the change can occur without pleasure, as when health returns gradually or when one is distracted from pleasurable sensations. The refutation concludes by diagnosing the error: taking the accidental (ʿaraḍ) for the essential (dhāt). Pain and pleasure require perception, and sensory perception, especially tactile, requires being affected (infiʿāl) by an opposite; when the quality stabilizes, no affection occurs, hence no perception, and thus no pleasure or pain.

Key Terms
ابن سينا
الانفعال
الإدراك الحسي
ما بالذات
ما بالعرض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If a change from unnatural to natural occurs, pleasure must automatically follow.
Correction: The refutation shows that change can occur without pleasure if perception is missing, e.g., gradual health or distraction.

Checkpoint
What example does Ibn Sīnā give to show that pleasure can occur without a preceding unnatural state?
A) Gradual health recovery
B) First-time tasting sweetness
C) Eating when hungry
D) Viewing beauty after longing

Key Takeaway
Pleasure requires perception; the change from unnatural to natural is accidental, not essential, to pleasure.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Ibn Sīnā argues that pleasure requires perception. Reflect on a recent moment of joy you experienced. Were you fully aware of it at the time, or did you only notice it later? How can you train yourself to be more present to the blessings Allah gives you, so that you truly perceive them and give thanks?
?
The refutation shows that change can occur without pleasure, like gradual recovery of health. Think of an area in your life where you are slowly improving (e.g., in prayer, character, or knowledge) but do not feel the pleasure of progress. What subtle signs of improvement might you be overlooking? How can you cultivate gratitude for these small, consistent changes?
?
The error in the theory is taking the accidental for the essential. Examine your own practice of Islam: are there rituals or habits you treat as the essence of faith when they are only accidental? What is truly essential in your relationship with Allah, and how can you refocus on it?

Pleasure and Pain: Sensory or Intellectual?
Sanusi distinguishes linguistic and customary usage of pleasure/pain and addresses an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the terms pleasure and pain extend from sensory to intellectual through common usage, and how this is reconciled with the objection about conceptual heat.

Core Idea
Pleasure and pain are not just sensory—common usage extends them to intellectual perception.

In this passage, Sanusi tackles a key terminological issue: do words like pleasure and pain only refer to physical sensations, or can they apply to intellectual experiences? He clarifies that while strict language limits them to the sensory, common usage broadens their scope. He then addresses a potential objection about conceiving heat without feeling pain. Let's examine his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the flow: first a statement about linguistic vs customary usage, then an objection and response.
Pay attention to how 'perception' (idrak) is used as a broader category.
2
Identify the two domains: language and custom
Distinguish what Sanusi says about 'language' (lugha) vs 'common usage' (urf). Language restricts pleasure/pain to sensory, but custom extends them.
3
Understand the objection and its reply
The objection says one can intellectually conceive of heat without feeling pain. Sanusi replies that the conception is just a mental image of heat, not the actual sensation that causes pain.
The term 'conforming form' (sura mutabiqa) means the mental representation matches the object, but it is not the object itself.
4
Reflect on the bigger picture
This clarification is important for Aqeedah because it allows the use of pleasure/pain language for spiritual and intellectual experiences, e.g., the pleasure of knowing Allah.
This connects to later discussions about the Beatific Vision and the pleasures of Paradise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا شك أن لفظ اللذة أو الألم بحسب اللغة إنما هو للحسي دون العقلي ، وأما بحسب العرف فالظاهر أنه بحسب المعنوي حيث يؤخذ الإدراك أعم من الإحساس والتعقل، ولا يرد الاعتراض بأن المدقوق [؟] قد يتعقل أن فيه حرارة غير طبيعية ولا يتألم لأن الحاصل بهذا التعقل صورة الحرارة المطابقة فهو إدراك
There is no doubt that the term pleasure or pain, according to language, is only for the sensory, not the intellectual. But according to common usage, it is apparent that it is according to the meaning, where perception is taken as more general than sensation and intellection. And the objection is not refuted that the careful [investigator] may conceive that there is an unnatural heat in it and not feel pain, because what is obtained by this conception is the conforming form of heat, so it is a perception.
Source pages p. 368

Commentary
This passage is a linguistic and terminological clarification. It states that in strict language (lugha), the words pleasure (ladhdha) and pain (alam) refer only to sensory experiences. However, in common usage (urf), they are applied to intellectual experiences as well, because perception (idrak) is understood in a broader sense encompassing both sensation (ihsas) and intellection (ta'aqqul). The passage then addresses a potential objection: a careful investigator might intellectually conceive of an unnatural heat without feeling pain. The reply is that what is obtained through that conception is just the conforming form (sura mutabiqa) of heat, which is a perception (idrak) but not the actual sensation that would cause pain.

Key Terms

عرف (common usage)
إدراك (perception)
إحساس (sensation)
تعقل (intellection)
صورة مطابقة (conforming form)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Pleasure and pain are only physical sensations and cannot be applied to thoughts or understanding.
Correction: While strict language limits them to sensory experience, common usage extends them to intellectual perception, as the terms are understood broadly.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The terms pleasure and pain are extended to intellectual experiences by common usage, as perception encompasses both sensation and intellection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
When you encounter a difficult concept in your deen that causes you mental discomfort—perhaps challenging a long-held assumption—do you allow yourself to feel that intellectual pain as a signal to re-evaluate, or do you dismiss it as irrelevant because it's 'just in your head'? What would change if you took it seriously as a genuine pain that demands a response?
?
The passage notes that common usage extends pleasure and pain to intellectual experiences. Consider your own usage: do you speak of the pain of regret or the pleasure of understanding as real? If not, could it be that you are undervaluing the spiritual and intellectual dimensions of your existence? Pledge today to recognize and respond to these experiences as real as physical ones.

Sensory Pleasure and Pain: Sensible or Psychical?
The author refutes the objection that sensory pleasure/pain are sensible qualities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why sensory pleasure and pain are classified as psychical qualities rather than sensible qualities, and how the author distinguishes between the cause of pleasure/pain and the pleasure/pain itself.

Core Idea
Pleasure and pain are perceptions, not qualities sensed by the external senses.

The author anticipates an objection: since we experience pleasure and pain through the senses, shouldn't they be considered sensible qualities? He responds with a crucial distinction—the sweet taste that causes pleasure is sensible, but the pleasure itself is a psychical perception. In this lesson, we'll unpack his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation. Let the author's argument flow naturally before you dissect it.
Pay attention to the objection and the response.
2
Identify the objection
Find the sentence where an opposing view is presented. What claim is being made?
The objection is introduced by 'فإن قيل'.
3
Understand the author's response
Notice how the author distinguishes between the quality that causes pleasure (e.g., sweetness) and the pleasure itself. Why does he say pleasure is not sensible?
The response begins with 'قلنا'.
4
Reflect on the classification
Based on the author's distinction, explain why pleasure and pain belong to psychical qualities rather than sensible qualities.
Consider the role of perception (إدراك) and attainment (نيل).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اللذات التابعة لهما وبحسب هذا تعرف حال الآلام عند التنبه لفقد الكمالات وأما أن العالم قد لا يلتذ بالإدراكات، ولا يتألم بالجهالات فعله لانتفاء بعض الشروط، والقيود المعتبرة في كون الإدراك لذة أو ألماً. فإن قيل : الحسي من اللذة والألم ينبغي أن يعد من الكيفيات المحسوسة دون الكيفيات النفسانية. قلنا : المدرك بالحس هو الكيفية التي يلتذ بها أو يتألم كالحلاوة والمرارة مثلاً . وأما نفس اللذة والألم التي هي من جنس الإدراك والنيل فلا سبيل للحواس الظاهرة إلى إدراكها .
The pleasures that follow from them and according to this one knows the states of pains upon realizing the loss of perfections. And as for that the scholar may not take pleasure in perceptions, nor be pained by ignorances, that is due to the absence of some conditions and restrictions considered in a perception being a pleasure or a pain. For it is said: The sensory aspect of pleasure and pain should be counted among sensible qualities, not among psychical qualities. We say: That which is perceived by the sense is the quality through which one takes pleasure or is pained, like sweetness and bitterness for example. As for the pleasure and pain themselves, which are of the genus of perception and attainment, the external senses have no way to perceive them.
Source pages p. 368

Commentary
This passage refutes a potential objection and clarifies the nature of sensory pleasure and pain. The author begins by stating that pleasures are dependent on certain conditions, and pain arises from realizing the loss of perfections. A scholar may not experience pleasure from perceptions or pain from ignorance due to missing conditions. Then he presents an objection: sensory pleasure and pain should be considered sensible qualities (like sweetness) rather than psychical qualities. The author responds by distinguishing between the sensible quality that causes pleasure/pain (e.g., sweetness, bitterness) and the actual pleasure/pain itself, which is a type of perception (idrak) and attainment (nayl) that the external senses cannot perceive. Thus, pleasure and pain are psychical, not sensible qualities.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Sensory pleasure and pain are themselves sensible qualities like color or taste.
Correction: Pleasure and pain are psychical perceptions of those qualities, not the qualities themselves. The external senses perceive only the qualities that cause pleasure/pain.

Checkpoint

C) Both

Key Takeaway
Pleasure and pain are psychical perceptions, not sensible qualities, because the external senses perceive only the qualities that cause them.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Ibn Sina's Definition of Health: Disposition or State
Definition of health by Ibn Sina and its genus as a psychological quality

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn Sina's definition of health as encompassing both stable dispositions (malaka) and transient states (hāl), with its genus being a psychological quality.

Core Idea
Health is a psychological quality, whether firm or fleeting.

Ibn Sina's definition of health is more inclusive than many assume. In this passage, he clarifies that health can be either a stable disposition or a temporary state, as long as it enables the body to act soundly. This refines our understanding of how kalam and medicine conceptualise bodily well-being.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Note the structure: introduction, definition, explanation, refutation, quote.
Pay attention to the terms malaka and hāl.
2
Identify key terms
Find the Arabic words for disposition (malaka), state (hāl), and psychological quality (kayfiyya nafsāniyya). Notice how they relate.
3
Understand the genus
The passage states that the genus of health is psychological quality, not limited to the firm disposition. Reflect on why this matters.
This refutes a restrictive view.
4
Apply the concept
Consider an example: a temporary feeling of wellness (hāl) counts as health by Ibn Sina's definition. This broadens the usual understanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: منها الصحة والمرض. أما الصحة فعرفها ابن سينا بأنها ملكة أو حال تصدر عنها الأفعال الموضوع لها سليمة يعني أن جنسها الكيفية النفسانية سواء كانت بصفة الرسوخ أو دونها لا كما هو رأي البعض من تخصيصها بالراسخة على ما قال في الشفاء ملكة في الجسم الحيواني تصدر عنه لأجلها الأفعال غير فارقة
He said: among them are health and disease. As for health, Ibn Sina defined it as a disposition (malaka) or a state (hāl) from which the actions of the subject issue soundly – meaning that its genus is the psychological quality (al-kayfiyya al-nafsāniyya), whether it is of the attribute of firmness (al-rusūkh) or below it, not as some opine who restrict it to the firm one, as he said in al-Shifā: a disposition in the animal body from which actions issue on its account, not separating.
Source pages p. 374

Commentary
This passage provides Ibn Sina's definition of health from his work al-Shifā (The Cure). Health is defined as a 'malaka' or 'hāl', meaning a disposition or a state, from which the actions of the subject (the body) proceed in a sound manner. The explanation clarifies that the genus (jins) of health is 'psychological quality' (al-kayfiyya al-nafsāniyya), which includes both stable dispositions (rāsikha) and less stable states. This refutes the view of some who restrict health only to the firm disposition. The reference to al-Shifā specifies that health is a disposition in the animal body from which actions issue on account of it, without separation (ghayr fāriqa), meaning the actions are not impeded.

Disease as Contrary or Privation: Defining a Kalam Term
Definition of disease as either contrary disposition or privation of health

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two ways disease is defined in kalam terminology: as a contrary disposition or state, or as a privation of health.

Core Idea
Disease can be either a positive contrary quality or the mere absence of health.

In kalam discussions on the soul, terms like health and disease are carefully defined. This passage explains that disease can be understood in two distinct ways: as a disposition or state opposed to health, or simply as the lack of health. The author also notes the genus of these qualities and cautions against loose usage.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation. Notice the repeated use of 'تارة' (sometimes) indicating two distinct usages.
2
Identify the two definitions
The passage presents two definitions: disease as a contrary disposition/state, and disease as a privation of health. Locate these in the text.
The Arabic phrases are 'ملكة أو حالة مضادة للصحة' and 'عدم ملكة لها'.
3
Understand the basis for dual usage
The text explains that the term may refer to the removal of health (zawāl al-ṣiḥḥa) or to what occurs from the principle of defect in actions.
4
Consider the taxonomic note
If we assume contrariety, both health and disease are psychological qualities. However, some enumerations loosely classify them as sensed things or non-qualities – this is leniency (tasāmuḥ).
This shows the precision expected in kalam terminology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المرض فجعله تارة ملكة أو حالة مضادة للصحة، وتارة عدم ملكة لها بناء على أنه قد يطلق على زوال الصحة، وقد يطلق على ما يحدث عنده من المبدأ للآفة في الأفعال، وعلى تقدير التضاد فهما من جنس الكيفية النفسانية وقد يذكر عند تعداد أنواعهما ما يدل على أن كليهما أو المرض خاصة من قبيل المحسوسات أو غير الكيفيات وهو تسامح
And as for disease, he sometimes made it a disposition (malaka) or a state (hāl) contrary to health, and sometimes a privation of it, based on the fact that it may be applied to the removal of health, and may be applied to what occurs from the principle of defect in actions. And on the assumption of contrariety, they are both of the genus of psychological quality. And sometimes when enumerating their types, there is mentioned what indicates that both of them, or disease in particular, are among the sensed things or non-qualities, and that is tolerance.
Source pages p. 374

Commentary
This passage defines disease (al-maraḍ) by presenting two ways it is used. First, disease can be considered a 'malaka' (disposition) or 'hāl' (state) that is contrary (muḍādda) to health. Second, it can be considered a 'private' ('adam malaka or 'adam hāl) of health, meaning the absence of the disposition or state of health. This dual usage comes from the fact that the term 'disease' may refer to the removal of health (zawāl al-ṣiḥḥa) or to the resulting impairment of actions. If we take disease as a contrary quality (tadād), then both health and disease belong to the genus of psychological quality (kayfiyya nafsāniyya). However, the author notes that sometimes when enumerating types of health and disease, one may find indications that both (or at least disease) are among sensed things (maḥsūsāt) or non-qualities, but this is considered leniency (tasāmuḥ) in terminology.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Disease is always a positive quality that exists as a contrary to health.
Correction: Disease can also be a mere privation (absence) of health, not a positive quality.

Checkpoint

A) As a contrary disposition/state or as a privation of health
B) As a harm or as a pain
C) As a bodily humor imbalance or as a spiritual affliction
D) As a sensed object or as a quality

Key Takeaway
Disease in kalam can refer either to a quality contrary to health or to the simple absence of health.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This passage shows that disease can be seen either as a positive quality or as a privation. In your own life, do you tend to view your shortcomings as active negative forces (like a contrary quality) or as mere absences of good? Which perspective leads you to take more responsibility?
?
The text notes that some scholars leniently classify disease as a sensed thing. Do you ever reduce your spiritual ailments to mere physical sensations, avoiding the deeper reality that they are qualities of the heart? When will you stop making excuses and confront your inner diseases as they truly are?
?
If health is a disposition from Allah, and disease is its absence, what does that say about your reliance on Him for every moment of well-being? Are you living as if health is a permanent possession or as a continuous gift that can be withdrawn?

Preference for malaka over hal in defining health and refutation of plant health
Argument for preferring malaka and refuting plant health

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the term 'malaka' (stable disposition) is preferred over 'hāl' (transient state) in the definition of health, and why health cannot be attributed to plants.

Core Idea
Health is a psychological quality, so plants cannot have health.

This lesson unpacks a nuanced debate in Islamic philosophy about the proper definition of health. The author argues for prioritizing 'malaka' (a firm, settled disposition) over 'hāl' (a temporary state) and rejects the extension of health to plants. By examining Ibn Sina's definitions and the rationale behind preferring malaka, you will grasp the subtle distinctions that shape theological and medical concepts.

Guided Path
1

Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation to grasp the flow of the argument.
The passage ends mid-sentence; the final point about plant health is incomplete.
2
Identify the main claim and supporting reasons
The author states that malaka is preferred because it is nobler, more prevalent, and agreed upon as health. Also note the refutation of plant health based on the psychological nature of health.
3
Analyze the definitions of Ibn Sina
Look at how Ibn Sina defines health in al-Qānūn and al-Shifā. Note the use of 'or' between malaka and hāl, and the explanation that the difference is accidental (firmness).
4
Understand the argument about plants
The author argues that health cannot apply to plants because it is a psychological quality (kayfiyya nafsāniyya), which plants lack. The passage ends while making this point.

5
Reflect on the significance of the malaka/hāl distinction
The lesson highlights that a difference in accident (firmness) does not affect the essence of health. This shows how careful conceptual analysis is essential in kalam.

وقدم الملكة لأنها أشرف وأغلب والمتفق على كونها صحة، وفيما زعم الإمام من شمولها صحة النبات ذهول عن معنى الملكة والحالة، وأما تخصيصها بالإنسان فيما قال : إنها هيئة يكون بها بدن الإنسان في مزاجه وتركيبه بحيث يصدر عنه الأفعال كلها صحيحة سليمة، فبالنظر إلى أنها المبحوث عنها في الطب، والمراد بالصحة والسلامة المعنى اللغوي بدليل الإسناد إلى الأفعال فلا دور . والدلالة بكلمتي عن ومن على مبدئية كل من الحال والمحل مبنية على أن الأول فاعلي والثاني مادي أو الأول آلي والثاني فاعلي . أي من الكيفيات النفسانية الصحة والمرض. أما الصحة فقد عرفها ابن سينا في أول القانون بأنها : ملكة أو حالة يصدر عنها الأفعال من الموضوع لها سليمة وليست كلمة أو لترديد المنافي للتحديد، بل الصحة هو الكيفية النفسانية سواء كانت راسخة أو غير جنس للتنبية على أن راسخة ، ولا يختص بالراسخة كما زعم البعض على ما قال في الشفاء إنها ملكة في الجسم الحيواني يصدر عنه لأجلها أفعاله الطبيعية وغيرها على المجرى الطبيعي غير مألوفة فأورد ما هو صحة بالإنفاق وهذا ما قيل : إن جنسها هو المسمى بالحال أو الملكة، وليس هناك شك في ذاتي للصحة ولا في عرضي على ما قال الإمام إنه لا يلزم من الشك في اندراج الصحة بحسب الحال أو الملكة شك في شيء من مقومات الصحة، بل في بعض عوارضها. لأن المخالفة بين الحال والملكة إنما هي بعارض الرسوخ وعدمه وإنما قدم الملكة على الحال في الذكر مع أنها متأخرة عنه في الوجود حيث تكون الكيفية أولاً حالاً ثم تصير ملكة لأن الملكة لرسوخها أشرف من الحال، ولأنها أغلب في الصحة وقال الإمام : لأنها لم يقع اختلاف في كونها صحة بخلاف الحال ولأنها غاية الحال، والغاية متقدمة في العلية وهذا التعريف يتناول صحة الإنسان وغيره من الحيوانات وما ذكر الإمام من أنه يتناول صحة النبات أيضاً وهو ما إذا كان أفعاله من الجذب والهضم سليمة ليس بمستقيم لأن الحال والملكة
And he gave precedence to malaka because it is nobler and more prevalent and agreed upon as being health. And the indication by the two words 'an and min of the principiality of each of the state and the locus is based on either the first being agent and the second material, or the first being instrumental and the second agent. That is, from the psychological qualities are health and disease. As for health, Ibn Sina defined it in the beginning of al-Qānūn as: a disposition (malaka) or a state (hāl) from which actions issue from the subject soundly, and the word 'or' is not for hesitation negating definition, but health is the psychological quality whether firm or not, as a genus for alerting that it is firm, and it is not specific to the firm as some alleged, as he said in al-Shifā: it is a disposition in the animal body from which on its account its natural and other actions issue in the natural course, not usual, so he presented what is health by consensus. And this is what was said: its genus is what is called hāl or malaka, and there is no doubt in the essence of health nor in its accident, according to what the Imam said: doubt about whether health falls under hāl or malaka does not entail doubt in any of the constituents of health, but in some of its accidents. Because the difference between hāl and malaka is only in the accident of firmness (rusūkh) and its absence. And he gave precedence to malaka over hāl in mention, even though it is later in existence, since the quality first is a hāl then becomes malaka, because malaka due to its firmness is nobler than hāl, and because it is more prevalent in health. And the Imam said: because no disagreement occurred about it being health, unlike hāl, and because it is the goal of hāl, and the goal is prior in causality. And this definition covers the health of humans and other animals, and what the Imam mentioned that it includes the health of plants as well – that is if its actions of attraction and digestion are sound – is not correct, because hāl and malaka...
Source pages p. 374

Commentary
This passage is a continuous argument within a commentary on the definition of health. It explains why the term 'malaka' (stable disposition) is preferred over 'hāl' (transient state) in the definition. The author states that malaka is preferred because it is nobler (ashraf), more prevalent (aghlab), and universally agreed upon as health. He then refutes the claim of al-Imām (likely al-Ghazālī or Fakhr al-Dīn al-Rāzī) that the definition includes plant health, calling it a neglect of the meanings of malaka and hāl, which are psychological qualities. Next, he discusses that some restrict health to humans because medicine investigates human health, but the linguistic meaning of health allows its use for animals as well, with no circularity. The prepositions 'an (from) and min (from) indicate principiality: either the state as agent and the locus as material, or state as instrumental and locus as agent. The text then repeats and expands on Ibn Sina's definition from al-Qānūn, emphasizing that 'or' is not for hesitation but for covering both types. It further explains that the genus is psychological quality, not limited to the firm. The discussion clarifies that doubt about whether health is a hāl or malaka does not affect its essential definition; it only concerns an accident (firmness). The reasons for prioritizing malaka are elaborated: malaka is nobler, more prevalent, agreed upon, and is the goal (ghāya) of hāl. The passage concludes by rejecting the inclusion of plants, arguing that plants do not have psychological qualities (hāl and malaka in the relevant sense), so the definition cannot apply to them. The text ends mid-sentence, likely continuing the refutation.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think health can be attributed to plants because they exhibit growth and digestion.

Ibn Sina's Definitions of Health and Illness
Ibn Sina defines health and illness and the author notes an inconsistency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn Sina's definitions of health and illness and the logical tension introduced by his dual characterization of illness.

Core Idea
Ibn Sina defines health as a disposition; illness as both a contrary temperament and pure non-existence.

This passage presents Ibn Sina's definitions of health and illness from his works Qanun and Shifa'. The author notes a critical tension: Ibn Sina describes illness both as a contrary temperament (like health) and as pure non-existence. This duality sets the stage for al-Razi's objection and the subsequent reconciliation.

Guided Path
1
Read the definitions carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the phrasing: health is 'malaka or hala' from which actions proceed soundly; illness is a 'contrary temperament' from which actions proceed unsoundly.
Pay attention to the terms 'malaka' and 'hala'.
2
Identify the tension
Ibn Sina also says illness in reality is pure non-existence. How can illness be both a temperament and non-existence? This is the logical problem.
3
Understand the initial indication of opposition
The passage ends by noting that the two statements indicate an opposition of privation and possession between health and illness.

The statement of the Qanun in the second definition, and clearer than it is the statement of the Shifa' because the lām is clearer in causation than the bā', and it is from [?] such that the repulsion of the objection about it is extremely evident. The Imam only raised it against the first statement. Thus what is mentioned in al-Mawaqif that health is a disposition (malaka) or state (hāla) from which actions proceed from the subject soundly, and that the Imam raised this objection against it is not as it should be. As for illness, Ibn Sina defined it as a contrary temperament to health, i.e., a disposition or state from which actions proceed from the subject unsoundly. And he mentioned in a place of the Shifa' that illness, insofar as it is illness in reality, is pure non-existence, I do not mean insofar as it is a bad temperament or pain. This indicates that between them is the opposition of possession and privation. The reconciliation between his two statements, as indicated by the Imam, is that health in his view is a temperament that is a principle for soundness of actions, and upon illness that temperament ceases, and a temperament occurs that is a principle for defect in actions. If illness is made an expression for the non-existence of the first temperament and its cessation, then between them is opposition of privation and possession. If it is made an expression for the second temperament itself, then it is opposition of contrariety. It is as if he intends that the word 'illness' is shared between the two matters, or is literal in one and metaphorical in the other. Otherwise the problem remains. And it was said that what is meant is that between them is opposition of privation and possession according to investigation, which is the special custom as before, or opposition of contrariety according to common usage, which is the general custom, because what is well-known is that two contraries are two matters that are attributed to one subject and cannot coexist, like evenness and oddness, not according to investigation as this would require them to be existing, differing under one proximate genus. And Ibn Sina explicitly stated this where he said: Indeed one of the two contraries in well-known contrariety may be a non-existence of the other, like rest for motion and illness for health. But his saying 'contrary temperament' perhaps indicates that illness is also existent like health, and there is no hidden fact that there is extreme difference between them, so it is permissible that they be made contraries according to investigation, falling under a genus which is the psychic quality. And the Imam objected that they agreed that the genera of simple diseases are three: bad temperament, bad structure, and discontinuity of continuity, and none of them falls under the psychic quality called state or disposition. As for bad temperament, it is either the strange quality itself by which the temperament left moderation, as is explicitly stated where it is said 'fever is such-and-such heat' and it is from the sensible qualities, or the body's being described by it, which is from the category of being affected. As for bad structure, it is an expression for magnitude or number or position or shape or obstruction of a passage disrupting actions, and none of them falls under state and disposition, and likewise the body's being described by them. This is because magnitude and number are from quantities, position is a category by itself, shape is from the qualities specific to quantities, and the description is from being affected; and he did not address obstruction, as if he makes it from position or being affected. As for discontinuity of continuity, it is non-existential and does not fall under any category at all. Therefore, since illness does not fall under state and disposition, health does not fall under it because it is contrary to it. This is the gist of the Imam's exposition, not what is mentioned in al-Mawaqif that bad temperament, bad structure, and discontinuity of continuity are either from the sensible...
Source pages p. 377

Commentary
This passage is a discussion of the definitions of health (ṣiḥḥa) and illness (marad) according to Ibn Sina, followed by an objection from Imam Fakhr al-Din al-Razi. The author first notes that the definition in the Qanun is clearer than that in the Shifa' because the letter lām in the Arabic phrase indicates causation more clearly than the bā'. He then states that the Imam's objection was directed at the first definition, though the objection recorded in al-Mawaqif is not appropriate. Ibn Sina defines health as a disposition (malaka) or state (ḥāla) from which actions proceed soundly, and illness as a contrary temperament (hay'a muḍādda) from which actions proceed unsoundly. However, in another place he says illness in reality is pure non-existence (ʿadam maḥḍ), indicating an opposition of privation and possession (taqābul al-ʿadam wa al-malaka) between health and illness. The author reconciles these statements: health is a temperament that is a principle for sound actions; upon illness that temperament ceases and a new temperament arises that is a principle for defective actions. If illness is defined as the absence of the first temperament, the opposition is privation vs. possession; if it is defined as the second temperament itself, it is contrariety (taḍādd). The word 'illness' may be used in both senses. The author then notes that according to custom, the distinction may be based on technical vs. popular usage. Ibn Sina explicitly states that one contrary in well-known contrariety may be the non-existence of the other. The passage concludes with al-Razi's objection: the three genera of simple diseases—bad temperament (sūʾ al-mizāj), bad structure (sūʾ al-tarkīb), and discontinuity of continuity (tafarruq al-ittiṣāl)—do not fall under the psychic quality (al-kayfiyya al-nafsāniyya) called state or disposition (al-ḥāl wa al-malaka). He argues each category belongs to other Aristotelian categories: bad temperament is a sensible quality or being affected; bad structure involves quantity, position, shape, or being affected; discontinuity is non-existential. Therefore, since illness is not under state or disposition, health is also excluded as its contrary. This is the gist of al-Razi's refutation, contrary to what is mentioned in al-Mawaqif.

Correction: Ibn Sina suggests illness can be considered as both a contrary temperament and as a privation, leading to different types of opposition.

Ibn Sina's definitions of health and illness contain a tension: illness is both a contrary temperament and pure non-existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Which view would make you more urgently seek repentance?

Ibn Sina's analysis suggests that privation is not a real quality—so your lack of good deeds is a void. Will you fill it?

Reconciling Ibn Sina's Statements on Illness and Types of Opposition
The author reconciles Ibn Sina's statements and discusses the types of opposition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author reconciles Ibn Sina's two characterizations of illness and the two types of opposition involved.

Core Idea
Illness can be seen as either privation of health or a contrary temperament, depending on perspective.

Having noted the tension in Ibn Sina's definitions, the author now proposes a reconciliation. He argues that illness can be understood in two ways: as the absence of the health temperament, which is an opposition of privation and possession, or as a new temperament itself, which is an opposition of contrariety. This distinction resolves the apparent contradiction and leads into a discussion of technical vs. popular usage.

Guided Path
1
Read the reconciliation
Read the passage from 'ووجه التوفيق بين كلاميه' to the point where the author discusses the two possibilities. Notice the phrases 'إن جعل المرض عبارة عن عدم الهيئة الأولى وزوالها' and 'إن جعل عبارة عن نفس الهيئة الثانية'.
These are the two interpretations.
2
Understand the two types of opposition
Opposition of privation and possession (taqābul al-ʿadam wa al-malaka) vs. opposition of contrariety (taqābul al-taḍādd). The first is when one thing is the absence of the other; the second is when both are existent but opposite.
Ibn Sina himself notes this distinction.
3
Note the role of custom and the psychic quality
The author mentions that according to technical custom (al-ʿurf al-khāṣṣ) the opposition is privation, while according to common usage it is contrariety. Also, both can fall under the genus 'psychic quality' (al-kayfiyya al-nafsāniyya).
This sets up al-Razi's objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عبارة القانون (۱) في التعريف الثاني، وأوضح منه عبارة الشفاء لأن اللام أوضح في التعليل من الباء، وهي من عن فاندفاع الاعتراض عنها في غاية الظهور والإمام إنما أورده على العبارة الأولى فما ذكر في المواقف أن الصحة ملكة أو حالة تصدر بها الأفعال عن الموضوع لها سليمة . وأن الإمام أورد عليه هذا الاعتراض ليس على ما ينبغي وأما المرض فقد عرفه ابن سينا بأنه هيئة مضادة للصحة أي ملكة أو حالة (۲) تصدر عنها الأفعال عن الموضوع لها غير سليمة، وذكر في موضع من الشفاء (۳) أن المرض من حيث هو مرض بالحقيقة فهو عدم محض (٤) لست أعني من حيث هو سوء مزاج أو ألم ، وهذا مشعر بأن بينهما تقابل الملكة والعدم، ووجه التوفيق بين كلاميه على ما أشار إليه الإمام، هو أن الصحة عنده هيئة هي مبدأ لسلامة الأفعال وعند المرض تزول تلك الهيئة، وتحدث هيئة هي مبدأ للآفة في الأفعال، فإن جعل المرض عبارة عن عدم الهيئة الأولى وزوالها فبينهما تقابل العدم والملكة ، وإن جعل عبارة عن نفس الهيئة الثانية فتقابل التضاد، وكأنه يريد أن لفظ المرض مشترك بين الأمرين أو حقيقة في أحدهما، مجاز في الآخر، وإلا فالإشكال بحاله . وقيل المراد أن بينهما تقابل العدم والملكة بحسب التحقيق وهو العرف الخاص على ما مر أو تقابل التضاد بحسب الشهرة، وهو العرف العامي لأن المشهور أن الضدين أمران ينسبان إلى موضوع واحد ولا يمكن أن يجتمعا كالزوجية والفردية لا بحسب التحقيق ليلزم كونهما موجودين في غاية التخلف تحت جنس واحد قريب (٦) وقد صرح بذلك ابن سينا حيث قال: إن أحد الضدين في التضاد المشهوري قد يكون عدماً للآخر كالسكون للحركة والمرض للصحة. لكن قوله هيئة مضادة ربما يشعر بأن المرض أيضاً وجودي كالصحة ولا خفاء في أن بينها غاية الخلاف، فجاز أن يجعلا ضدين بحسب التحقيق مندرجين تحت جنس هو الكيفية النفسانية . واعترض الإمام بأنهم اتفقوا على أن أجناس الأمراض (۱) المفردة ثلاثة : سوء المزاج، وسوء التركيب وتفرق الاتصال ولا شيء منها بداخل تحت الكيفية النفسانية المسماة بالحال أو الملكة. أما سوء المزاج فلأنه إما نفس الكيفية الغريبة التي بها خرج المزاج عن الاعتدال على ما يصرح به حيث يقال الحمى حرارة كذا وكذا وهي من الكيفيات المحسوسة، وإما اتصاف البدن بها وهو من مقولة أن ينفعل ، وأما سوء التركيب فلأنه عبارة عن مقدار أو عن عدد أو وضع أو شكل أو انسداد مجرى مخل بالأفعال، وليس شيء منها داخلاً تحت الحال والملكة، وكذا اتصاف البدن بها، وذلك لأن المقدار والعدد من الكميات والوضع مقولة (۲) برأسها ، والشكل من الكيفيات المختصة بالكميات والانصاف من أن ينفعل ولم يتعرض للانسداد، وكأنه يجعله من الوضع أو أن ينفعل ، وأما تفرق الاتصال فلأنه عـدمـي لا يدخل تحت مقولة أصلا، ولذا لم يدخل المرض تحت الحال والملكة لم يدخل الصحة تحتها لكونه ضداً لها. هذا حاصل (۳) تقرير الإمام. لا ما ذكر في المواقف من أن سوء المزاج ، وسوء التركيب وتفرق الاتصال. إما من المحسوسة
The statement of the Qanun in the second definition, and clearer than it is the statement of the Shifa' because the lām is clearer in causation than the bā', and it is from [?] such that the repulsion of the objection about it is extremely evident. The Imam only raised it against the first statement. Thus what is mentioned in al-Mawaqif that health is a disposition (malaka) or state (hāla) from which actions proceed from the subject soundly, and that the Imam raised this objection against it is not as it should be. As for illness, Ibn Sina defined it as a contrary temperament to health, i.e., a disposition or state from which actions proceed from the subject unsoundly. And he mentioned in a place of the Shifa' that illness, insofar as it is illness in reality, is pure non-existence, I do not mean insofar as it is a bad temperament or pain. This indicates that between them is the opposition of possession and privation. The reconciliation between his two statements, as indicated by the Imam, is that health in his view is a temperament that is a principle for soundness of actions, and upon illness that temperament ceases, and a temperament occurs that is a principle for defect in actions. If illness is made an expression for the non-existence of the first temperament and its cessation, then between them is opposition of privation and possession. If it is made an expression for the second temperament itself, then it is opposition of contrariety. It is as if he intends that the word 'illness' is shared between the two matters, or is literal in one and metaphorical in the other. Otherwise the problem remains. And it was said that what is meant is that between them is opposition of privation and possession according to investigation, which is the special custom as before, or opposition of contrariety according to common usage, which is the general custom, because what is well-known is that two contraries are two matters that are attributed to one subject and cannot coexist, like evenness and oddness, not according to investigation as this would require them to be existing, differing under one proximate genus. And Ibn Sina explicitly stated this where he said: Indeed one of the two contraries in well-known contrariety may be a non-existence of the other, like rest for motion and illness for health. But his saying 'contrary temperament' perhaps indicates that illness is also existent like health, and there is no hidden fact that there is extreme difference between them, so it is permissible that they be made contraries according to investigation, falling under a genus which is the psychic quality. And the Imam objected that they agreed that the genera of simple diseases are three: bad temperament, bad structure, and discontinuity of continuity, and none of them falls under the psychic quality called state or disposition. As for bad temperament, it is either the strange quality itself by which the temperament left moderation, as is explicitly stated where it is said 'fever is such-and-such heat' and it is from the sensible qualities, or the body's being described by it, which is from the category of being affected. As for bad structure, it is an expression for magnitude or number or position or shape or obstruction of a passage disrupting actions, and none of them falls under state and disposition, and likewise the body's being described by them. This is because magnitude and number are from quantities, position is a category by itself, shape is from the qualities specific to quantities, and the description is from being affected; and he did not address obstruction, as if he makes it from position or being affected. As for discontinuity of continuity, it is non-existential and does not fall under any category at all. Therefore, since illness does not fall under state and disposition, health does not fall under it because it is contrary to it. This is the gist of the Imam's exposition, not what is mentioned in al-Mawaqif that bad temperament, bad structure, and discontinuity of continuity are either from the sensible...
Source pages p. 377

Commentary
This passage is a discussion of the definitions of health (ṣiḥḥa) and illness (marad) according to Ibn Sina, followed by an objection from Imam Fakhr al-Din al-Razi. The author first notes that the definition in the Qanun is clearer than that in the Shifa' because the letter lām in the Arabic phrase indicates causation more clearly than the bā'. He then states that the Imam's objection was directed at the first definition, though the objection recorded in al-Mawaqif is not appropriate. Ibn Sina defines health as a disposition (malaka) or state (ḥāla) from which actions proceed soundly, and illness as a contrary temperament (hay'a muḍādda) from which actions proceed unsoundly. However, in another place he says illness in reality is pure non-existence (ʿadam maḥḍ), indicating an opposition of privation and possession (taqābul al-ʿadam wa al-malaka) between health and illness. The author reconciles these statements: health is a temperament that is a principle for sound actions; upon illness that temperament ceases and a new temperament arises that is a principle for defective actions. If illness is defined as the absence of the first temperament, the opposition is privation vs. possession; if it is defined as the second temperament itself, it is contrariety (taḍādd). The word 'illness' may be used in both senses. The author then notes that according to custom, the distinction may be based on technical vs. popular usage. Ibn Sina explicitly states that one contrary in well-known contrariety may be the non-existence of the other. The passage concludes with al-Razi's objection: the three genera of simple diseases—bad temperament (sūʾ al-mizāj), bad structure (sūʾ al-tarkīb), and discontinuity of continuity (tafarruq al-ittiṣāl)—do not fall under the psychic quality (al-kayfiyya al-nafsāniyya) called state or disposition (al-ḥāl wa al-malaka). He argues each category belongs to other Aristotelian categories: bad temperament is a sensible quality or being affected; bad structure involves quantity, position, shape, or being affected; discontinuity is non-existential. Therefore, since illness is not under state or disposition, health is also excluded as its contrary. This is the gist of al-Razi's refutation, contrary to what is mentioned in al-Mawaqif.

Key Terms
تقابل العدم والملكة
تقابل التضاد
الكيفية النفسانية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Privation and contrariety are the same type of opposition.
Correction: Privation is the absence of a quality; contrariety is between two existent positive qualities.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the type of opposition between health and illness if we consider illness as the absence of the health temperament?
A) Contrariety (taḍādd)
B) Privation and possession (ʿadam wa malaka)
C) Relational opposition
D) Contradiction

Key Takeaway
Ibn Sina's apparent contradiction is reconciled by distinguishing two senses of illness: privation of health or a contrary temperament.

Logic and Technical Terms
advanced
7 min
Al-Razi's Objection: The Three Genera of Simple Diseases
Al-Razi objects that the three genera of simple diseases do not fall under the category of state or disposition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Razi's argument that the three genera of simple diseases do not fall under the category of psychic quality, thus refuting Ibn Sina's classification.

Core Idea
Simple diseases belong to categories other than 'state and disposition', invalidating the psychic quality classification.

Al-Razi objects to the claim that health and illness fall under the psychic quality (al-kayfiyya al-nafsāniyya) called state or disposition. He argues that the three recognized genera of simple diseases—bad temperament, bad structure, and discontinuity of continuity—each belong to other Aristotelian categories. Therefore, illness cannot be classified as a psychic quality, and by extension health cannot either.

Guided Path
1
Read al-Razi's objection
Read the passage from 'واعترض الإمام' onwards. Al-Razi lists the three genera and argues why each does not fall under state/disposition.
2
Analyze the categories for each genus
For bad temperament: either a sensible quality or the body's being affected. For bad structure: involves quantity, position, shape, or being affected. For discontinuity: non-existential and not under any category.
3
Understand the conclusion
Since none of the disease genera fall under state/disposition, illness is not a psychic quality. Therefore, health, as its contrary, is also not a psychic quality. This refutes the classification.

Source Text

Commentary
This passage is a discussion of the definitions of health (ṣiḥḥa) and illness (marad) according to Ibn Sina, followed by an objection from Imam Fakhr al-Din al-Razi. The author first notes that the definition in the Qanun is clearer than that in the Shifa' because the letter lām in the Arabic phrase indicates causation more clearly than the bā'. He then states that the Imam's objection was directed at the first definition, though the objection recorded in al-Mawaqif is not appropriate. However, in another place he says illness in reality is pure non-existence (ʿadam maḥḍ), indicating an opposition of privation and possession (taqābul al-ʿadam wa al-malaka) between health and illness. The author reconciles these statements: health is a temperament that is a principle for sound actions; upon illness that temperament ceases and a new temperament arises that is a principle for defective actions. If illness is defined as the absence of the first temperament, the opposition is privation vs. possession; if it is defined as the second temperament itself, it is contrariety (taḍādd). The word 'illness' may be used in both senses. The author then notes that according to custom, the distinction may be based on technical vs. popular usage. Ibn Sina explicitly states that one contrary in well-known contrariety may be the non-existence of the other. The passage concludes with al-Razi's objection: the three genera of simple diseases—bad temperament (sūʾ al-mizāj), bad structure (sūʾ al-tarkīb), and discontinuity of continuity (tafarruq al-ittiṣāl)—do not fall under the psychic quality (al-kayfiyya al-nafsāniyya) called state or disposition (al-ḥāl wa al-malaka). He argues each category belongs to other Aristotelian categories: bad temperament is a sensible quality or being affected; bad structure involves quantity, position, shape, or being affected; discontinuity is non-existential. Therefore, since illness is not under state or disposition, health is also excluded as its contrary. This is the gist of al-Razi's refutation, contrary to what is mentioned in al-Mawaqif.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All diseases are psychic qualities like dispositions.
Correction: Al-Razi shows that the three genera belong to different categories: quality, quantity, position, being affected, or non-being.

Checkpoint
According to al-Razi, under which category does bad structure (sūʾ al-tarkīb) belong?
A) Quality
B) Quantity, position, or shape
C) Substance
D) Relation

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi refutes Ibn Sina's classification by demonstrating that the three disease genera do not fall under the category of state or disposition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you unconsciously treat your own physical ailments as mere 'states' or 'dispositions' that define you spiritually? Al-Razi's analysis shows they are not psychic qualities—how does that challenge your tendency to conflate health with spiritual status? Will you now separate your bodily conditions from your inner state?
?
Al-Razi argues that discontinuity of continuity (like a wound) is non-existential and thus not a category. When you experience a loss or break in your life, do you try to fit it into a mental category like 'state'? This lesson forces you to accept that some realities are not categorizable. Are you willing to let go of the need to define every experience?
?
This objection dismantles a key classification in Islamic philosophy. Have you ever held a comfortable belief about yourself or your faith that was actually based on a flawed categorization? What would it cost you to have that deconstructed like Al-Razi deconstructs Ibn Sina? Are you ready to question your own frameworks?

Imam al-Razi's Objection: Genera of Disease Are Not Psychical Qualities
Imam al-Razi enumerates and analyzes three genera of diseases to refute Ibn Sina.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam al-Razi argued that the three genera of diseases cannot fall under the category of state/disposition (al-hal wa al-malaka).

Core Idea
Disease's genera are physical, not mental states.

Imam al-Razi challenges Ibn Sina's claim that disease and health are contraries under psychical quality. He cites consensus on three simple genera of diseases—bad temperament, bad composition, and discontinuity of continuity—and demonstrates that each belongs to non-psychical categories. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Three Genera
Read the passage to find the three simple genera of diseases that scholars agreed upon. Note their names in Arabic and English.
They are: سوء المزاج, سوء التركيب, تفرق الاتصال.
2
Analyze Each Genus
For each genus, Imam al-Razi explains why it does not fall under state/disposition. Identify the category each belongs to (sensible quality, quantity, position, privation, etc.).
Bad temperament is either sensible or being affected; bad composition involves quantity, position, etc.; discontinuity is privative.
3
Understand the Conclusion
If disease does not fall under state/disposition, then health, as its contrary, also does not. Grasp the logical consequence.
This refutes Ibn Sina's classification.

ابن سينا حيث قال: إن أحد الضدين في التضاد المشهوري قد يكون عدماً للآخر كالسكون للحركة والمرض للصحة. لكن قوله هيئة مضادة ربما يشعر بأن المرض أيضاً وجودي كالصحة ولا خفاء في أن بينها غاية الخلاف، فجاز أن يجعلا ضدين بحسب التحقيق مندرجين تحت جنس هو الكيفية النفسانية. واعترض الإمام بأنهم اتفقوا على أن أجناس الأمراض المفردة ثلاثة: سوء المزاج، وسوء التركيب وتفرق الاتصال ولا شيء منها بداخل تحت الكيفية النفسانية المسماة بالحال أو الملكة. أما سوء المزاج فلأنه إما نفس الكيفية الغريبة التي بها خرج المزاج عن الاعتدال على ما يصرح به حيث يقال الحمى حرارة كذا وكذا وهي من الكيفيات المحسوسة، وإما اتصاف البدن بها وهو من مقولة أن ينفعل، وأما سوء التركيب فلأنه عبارة عن مقدار أو عن عدد أو وضع أو شكل أو انسداد مجرى مخل بالأفعال، وليس شيء منها داخلاً تحت الحال والملكة، وكذا اتصاف البدن بها، وذلك لأن المقدار والعدد من الكميات والوضع مقولة برأسها، والشكل من الكيفيات المختصة بالكميات والانصاف من أن ينفعل ولم يتعرض للانسداد، وكأنه يجعله من الوضع أو أن ينفعل، وأما تفرق الاتصال فلأنه عدمي لا يدخل تحت مقولة أصلا، ولذا لم يدخل المرض تحت الحال والملكة لم يدخل الصحة تحتها لكونه ضداً لها. هذا حاصل تقرير الإمام. لا ما ذكر في المواقف من أن سوء المزاج، وسوء التركيب وتفرق الاتصال. إما من المحسوسة أو الوضع أو عدم التركيب، فإنه اختصار مخل، والعذر بأنه لم يقيد بباقي المحتملات لظهور بطلانها ظاهر البطلان، لأن قولنا سوء التركيب إما مقدار يخل بالأفعال، أو عدد أو وضع أو انسداد مجرى كذلك ليس بياناً للمحتملات، بل للانقسام فليفهم، وتقرير الجواب بعد تسليم كون التضاد حقيقياً أن تقسيم المرض إلى سوء المزاج، وسوء التركيب وتفرق الاتصال تسامح، والمقصود أنه كيفية نفسانية تحصل عند هذه الأمور وتنقسم باعتبارها وهذا ما قيل إنها منوعات أطلق عليها اسم الأنواع، وذلك كما يطلق الصحة على اعتدال المزاج، أو المزاج المعتدل أنه من المحسوسات.
Ibn Sina said: 'One of the two contraries in well-known opposition may be the nonexistence of the other, like rest for motion and disease for health.' But his statement 'contradictory form' might suggest that disease is also existential like health, and there is no doubt that there is extreme difference between them, so it is possible that they are considered contraries according to investigation, falling under a genus which is the psychical quality. And the Imam objected that they [scholars] agreed that the simple genera of diseases are three: bad temperament, bad composition, and discontinuity of continuity, and none of them fall under the psychical quality called state or disposition. As for bad temperament, it is either the strange quality itself by which the temperament departed from moderation, as explicitly stated when one says 'the fever is such-and-such heat,' which is among the sensible qualities, or the body's being qualified by it, which is from the category of being affected. As for bad composition, it is an expression for quantity, or number, or position, or shape, or obstruction of a passage impairing actions, and none of these fall under state and disposition, similarly the body's being qualified by them, because quantity and number are from quantities, position is a separate category, shape is from the qualities specific to quantities, and [one could say] being qualified is from being affected, and he did not mention obstruction, as if he makes it from position or being affected. As for discontinuity of continuity, it is privative, not falling under any category at all. Therefore, since disease does not fall under state and disposition, health does not fall under it either because it is its contrary. This is the substance of the Imam's argument. Not what was mentioned in al-Mawaqif that bad temperament, bad composition, and discontinuity of continuity are either from the sensible or position or lack of composition, for it is a deficient abbreviation, and the excuse that he did not restrict it to the remaining possibilities because their falsity is apparent is clearly false, because our statement 'bad composition is either an amount impairing actions, or number, or position, or obstruction of a passage' is not an enumeration of possibilities, but a division, so understand. And the explanation of the answer after conceding that the opposition is real is that the division of disease into bad temperament, bad composition, and discontinuity of continuity is loose, and the intended meaning is that it is a psychical quality that occurs along with these matters and is divided by them, and this is what is said: they are varieties called kinds, just as health is applied to balanced temperament, or the moderate temperament being among the sensible [qualities].
Source pages p. 378

Commentary
This passage presents an objection by Imam al-Razi against Ibn Sina's classification of disease as a contrary of health under the genus of psychical quality (al-kayfiyya al-nafsaniyya), and then offers a counter-answer. First, Ibn Sina is cited: he considered disease and health as opposites where one is the privation of the other (like rest and motion), and despite their difference, they can be considered contraries under the same genus of psychical quality. Imam al-Razi objects by citing consensus that the simple genera of diseases are three: bad temperament (saw' al-mizaj), bad composition (saw' al-tarkib), and discontinuity of continuity (tafarruq al-ittisal). He argues none of these fall under the category of state/disposition (al-hal wa al-malaka). For each, he gives reasons: bad temperament is either a strange sensible quality or the body being affected; bad composition involves quantity, number, position, shape, or obstruction—none of which are mental states; discontinuity is privative, not under any category. Hence disease is not a psychical quality, and by extension health is not either. The author then criticizes a presentation in al-Mawaqif for being a deficient summary. Finally, a response is given: conceding that the opposition is real, the division into three genera is loose; the true intended meaning is that disease is a psychical quality that arises from these physical causes and is divided by them—these are just varieties called kinds, analogous to how 'health' is sometimes used for balanced temperament itself.

Key Terms
الحال والملكة
الكيفية النفسانية
سوء المزاج
سوء التركيب
تفرق الاتصال
التضاد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Disease and health are simple contraries under the same genus of psychical quality.
Correction: Imam al-Razi shows the genera of disease are physical or privative, not psychical, so the classification fails.

Checkpoint
Why does Imam al-Razi say discontinuity of continuity (tafarruq al-ittisal) does not fall under any category?
A) Because it is a sensible quality.
B) Because it is a form of bad composition.
C) Because it is a privation (adami), not a positive quality.
D) Because it is a state of the body.

Key Takeaway
The three genera of disease belong to categories like sensible, quantity, position, and privation—none is a psychical quality, so disease and health are not contraries under state/disposition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever mistakenly attributed a physical ailment to a purely spiritual or psychological cause, ignoring the physical reality? How does al-Razi's meticulous classification challenge you to examine the true nature of things before jumping to conclusions?
?
In your daily life, when you encounter opposites (like health and disease, strength and weakness), do you assume they are simply two sides of the same coin? This lesson shows that opposites may belong to entirely different categories. Where else might you be guilty of oversimplifying complex realities?
?
Al-Razi insists on precise logical division rather than vague possibilities. How often do you settle for 'abbreviated' understandings of truth, like the summary in al-Mawaqif, instead of diving into the detailed argument? What would it cost you to think carefully about your own beliefs?

The Response: Loose Classification and the True Intended Meaning
Response to Imam al-Razi's objection, explaining the division as loose and the true intended meaning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the counter-answer to Imam al-Razi's objection, which argues that the division into three genera is loose and the true genus is psychical quality arising from physical causes.

Core Idea
Disease's true genus is psychical, caused by physical genera.

After the objection, a response is given: the opponent concedes that the opposition is real but argues that the division of diseases into three genera is not strict—it is a loose classification (tasamuh). The intended meaning is that disease is a psychical quality that arises from these physical genera, akin to how health is sometimes called balanced temperament. This lesson explains that nuance.

Guided Path
1
Read the Response
Read the passage starting from 'وتقرير الجواب بعد تسليم' to the end. It explains how the division is loose.
The response concedes the opposition is real but reinterprets the classification.
2
Understand 'Loose Division'
The term 'tasamuh' means a lenient or loose division. Here, the genera are not strictly categories but varieties that act as causes for the psychical quality.
This is a key concept in Islamic logic and categorization.
3
Connect to the Health Analogy
Just as 'health' can be applied to balanced temperament (which is sensible), disease is applied to a psychical quality that accompanies physical conditions. Recognize the parallel.
This shows how a term can be used loosely for a related concept.

The Intermediate State Between Health and Disease
Sanusi clarifies the intermediate state between health and disease.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the kalām concept of the intermediate state between health and disease and the specific nature of the scholarly disagreement.

Core Idea
The intermediate state is a real bodily condition, not a mere conceptual continuum.

This passage from Sharḥ al-Maqāṣid examines a subtle but important kalām distinction: whether there exists a middle ground between health and disease. The author clarifies that the debate is not about states like knowledge or power, which are clearly neither health nor disease, but about a specific bodily condition that is neither fully healthy nor fully diseased. Let's explore how Galen and others understood this category.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once through, then the English translation. Notice the conditional opening and the clarification that follows.
The passage uses a conditional structure: 'if disease is a deficiency... then the intermediate state is established.'
2
Identify the key distinction
The author distinguishes between two types of intermediate states: general attributes like knowledge (which are not the focus) and a specific bodily state that is neither healthy nor diseased. Underline this distinction.
3
Note the examples from Galen
Galen provides examples: convalescents, the elderly, children, and those with a defect in some organs. Consider why each is neither fully healthy nor fully diseased.
These examples illustrate a real, affirmative intermediate state.
4
Reflect on the significance
Think about why this classification matters in kalām. It shows that not all states fit into binary categories, which has implications for theological and medical discussions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: ثم المعتبر في المرض إن كان يثبت الواسطة وإن كان آفة الجميع يثبت. قد اختلفوا في ثبوت الواسطة بين الصحة والمرض، وليس الخلاف في ثبوت حالة وصفة لا يصدق عليها الصحة ولا المرض كالعلم والقدرة والحياة إلى غير ذلك مما لا يحصى بل في ثبوت حالة لا يصدق معها على البدن أنه صحيح أو مريض، بل يصدق عليه أنه ليس بصحيح ولا مريض فأثبتها جالينوس كما للناقهين والمشايخ والأطفال، ومن ببعض أعضائه آفة.
He said: Then the considered [criterion] regarding disease: if it [the disease] is a deficiency, the intermediate state is established; and if it is a defect of the whole, it is established. They differed regarding the existence of the intermediate state between health and disease. The disagreement is not about the existence of a state or attribute that is not called health or disease, like knowledge, power, life, and countless others. Rather, it is about the existence of a state such that with it the body is not called healthy or diseased, but rather it is called neither healthy nor diseased. Galen affirmed it, as for convalescents, the elderly, children, and one who has a defect in some of his organs.
Source pages p. 379

Commentary
This passage discusses the possibility of an intermediate state (al-wasita) between health and disease. It begins by stating that if disease is considered a deficiency or a defect of the whole, the intermediate state is established. Then it clarifies the nature of the disagreement: scholars do not dispute the existence of states that are neither health nor disease in a general sense (like knowledge or power), which are qualities of the soul and not bodily states. Rather, the dispute is specifically about a bodily condition that is neither healthy nor diseased—that is, a state where the body is not accurately described as either 'healthy' or 'diseased', but as something in between. Galen affirmed this intermediate state, giving examples such as convalescents recovering from illness, the elderly, children, and those with a defect in only some organs. These individuals are not fully healthy nor fully diseased, establishing a real intermediate category.

Key Terms
الواسطة (al-wasita)
الصحة (al-sihha)
المرض (al-marad)
الناقهين (al-nāqihīn)
المشايخ (al-mashāyikh)
الأطفال (al-atfāl)
آفة (āfa)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the intermediate state must be a continuum between health and disease.
Correction: The passage clarifies it is a distinct, affirmative state, not a mere gradual transition.

Checkpoint
What exactly did scholars disagree about regarding the intermediate state (al-wasita) between health and disease?
A) Whether there are any states that are neither health nor disease.
B) Whether the body can be in a state that is neither healthy nor diseased.
C) Whether convalescents are healthy.
D) Whether disease is a privation.

Key Takeaway
The intermediate state is a real bodily condition affirmed by Galen, distinct from both health and disease.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about your own spiritual state, do you tend to label yourself as either 'faithful' or 'sinful'? This lesson shows that an intermediate state is real and acknowledged. Are you avoiding the discomfort of admitting you might be in that 'neither' zone—not fully devoted but not lost? What would change today if you honestly recognized that intermediate state and took one small step toward health?
?
The passage highlights nuanced disagreements over categories, cautioning against oversimplification. Where in your life do you reduce complex situations to binary choices (right/wrong, believer/unbeliever) to avoid wrestling with ambiguity? What is one relationship, belief, or habit you have forced into a binary that deserves the careful consideration Galen gave to health and disease?

Emotions Defined by Spirit Movement
Defines emotional states by their characteristic movements of the spirit.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how classical kalam defines emotional states through the movement of the spirit (الروح) rather than mere physical sensations.

Core Idea
Emotional states are defined by the direction of the spirit's movement in response to perception.

This passage introduces a unique way of defining emotions in Islamic theology. Unlike common descriptions that focus on physical sensations (like boiling blood for anger), the author defines each emotion by the movement of the spirit (الروح) either inward or outward. This approach reveals a refined psychological framework.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the list of emotions, then read the translation to understand the definitions.
Notice that each emotion is paired with a specific movement direction.
2
Identify the pattern of spirit movement
Observe how each emotion is defined by whether the spirit moves outward (seeking pleasure/revenge), inward (fearing harm), or both.
This pattern is the core of the author's analysis.
3
Distinguish simple vs compound emotions
Note that anxiety (الهم) and shame (الخجل) involve both inward and outward movements, making them compound emotions.
Anxiety is described as an intellectual struggle between hope and fear.
4
Reflect on the concluding remark
The author notes that common descriptions (like joy=expansion of heart) are lenient. Understand that the precise definitions are based on spirit movement, not these common metaphors.
This shows the technical precision of kalam terminology.
