Al-Mushtarak and Types of Commonality
Defines al-mushtarak and enumerates types of commonality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of al-mushtarak and the systematic classification of types of commonality (al-ishtirak) between things.

Core Idea
The way two things share a property determines the type of commonality.

In theological and philosophical discourse, precise terms like 'commonality' are essential for clear reasoning. This passage from al-Sanusi defines what a common term is and then systematically categorizes the ways two things can share a property. Understanding this taxonomy will sharpen your ability to analyze arguments that rely on analogies and comparisons.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of al-mushtarak
Start by reading the Arabic passage slowly. Notice how al-mushtarak is defined as a single word applied equally to different realities. The example of 'ayn (eye, spring, sun) illustrates this.
The term 'mushtarak' is distinct from 'ishtirak' (commonality).
2
Examine the two broad categories
The passage divides commonality (ishtirak) into two main types: by species (mumathala) and by genus (mujānasa). Identify these and their examples.
3
Break down the accidental commonalities
Then it lists commonality by accident, which splits into six sub-types: quantity (musāwā), quality (mushābaha), relation (munāsaba), shape, position (muwāzana), and extremities (mutābaqa). Note the specific condition for each.
The passage omits a specific name for shape but gives an example.
4
Reflect on why this taxonomy matters
Consider how each type of commonality is used in kalam arguments. For instance, resemblance (mushābaha) is often invoked in analogical reasoning (qiyas).
This passage is foundational for understanding later discussions on similitude.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المشترك هو اللفظ الواحد الذي يطلق على أشياء مختلفة بالحد والحقيقة اطلاقاً متساوياً كالعين تطلق على آلة البصر، ينبوع الماء، وقرص الشمس، وهذه مختلفة الحدود والحقائق. والاشتراك بين الشيئين، إن كان بالنوع يسمى مماثلة كاشتراك بين زيد وعمرو في الإنسانية، فإن كان بالجنس يسمى مجانسة كاشتراك إنسان وفرس في الحيوانية، وإن كان بالعرض، فإن كان في الكم يسمى مساواة، كاشتراك ذراع من خشب، وذراع من ثوب في الطول، وإن كان في الكيف يسمى مشابهة كاشتراك الإنسان والحجر في السواد، وإن كان بالمضاف يسمى مناسبة، كاشتراك زيد وعمرو في بنوة بكر، وإن كان بالشكل يسمى كاشتراك الأرض والهواء في الكرية، وإن كان بالوضع المخصوص يسمى موازنة، وهو أن لا يختلف البعد بينهما كسطح كل فلك، وإن كان بالأطراف يسمى مطابقة.
The common term (al-mushtarak) is the single word that is applied to different things in definition and reality equally, like 'ayn applied to the instrument of sight, the spring of water, and the disc of the sun, and these are different in definitions and realities. The commonality (al-ishtirak) between two things: if it is by species, it is called similitude (mumathala), like the commonality between Zayd and Amr in humanity; if by genus, it is called homogeneity (mujānasa), like the commonality between a human and a horse in animality; if by accident, then if in quantity, it is called equality (musāwā), like the commonality of a cubit of wood and a cubit of cloth in length; if in quality, it is called resemblance (mushābaha), like the commonality of a human and a stone in blackness; if by relation, it is called proportionality (munāsaba), like the commonality of Zayd and Amr in being sons of Bakr; if by shape, it is called such as the commonality of earth and air in sphericity; if by specific position, it is called balacing (muwāzana), which is that the distance between them does not differ, like the surface of each sphere; if by extremities, it is called congruence (mutābaqa).
Source pages p. 389

Commentary
This passage provides a definition of al-mushtarak (the common term) in logic and then enumerates the types of commonality (al-ishtirak) between two things. It begins by defining al-mushtarak as a single word applied to different realities equally, giving the example of 'ayn (eye, spring, sun). Then it classifies commonality based on the category of the shared attribute. The classification is systematic: if the commonality is in species, it is called similitude (mumathala); if in genus, homogeneity (mujānasa); if in accident, it branches into quantity (equality), quality (resemblance), relation (proportionality), shape (implicitly named after the example), specific position (balancing), and extremities (congruence). The passage thus serves as a comprehensive taxonomy of the ways two things can be considered common, crucial for precise theological and philosophical discourse.

Correction: The passage clearly distinguishes al-mushtarak (the common term) from al-ishtirak (the relation of commonality). Al-mushtarak is a word, while al-ishtirak is the commonality between two things.

Dispositional Qualities: Definition and Subtypes
Al-Taftazani defines dispositional qualities and their two subtypes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of dispositional qualities and their two subtypes: disposition to be affected (al-lāqūwa) and disposition to resist (al-qūwa).

Core Idea
Dispositional qualities are strong inclinations to either receive or resist effects.

Al-Taftazani now turns to the fourth division of quality: dispositional qualities. These are not static properties but readinesses within a thing. Understanding them requires grasping two opposite tendencies: the readiness to be affected and the readiness to resist. This passage defines these two subtypes and gives a common term that covers both.

Guided Path
1
Read and identify the division
Read the Arabic passage once. Notice that it begins by naming the fourth division of quality: dispositional qualities (الكيفيات الاستعدادية).
This division is part of a larger classification of qualities.
2
Distinguish the two subtypes
Find the two types: disposition to be affected (e.g., sickliness, softness) called al-lāqūwa, and disposition to resist (e.g., healthiness, hardness) called al-qūwa.
Al-lāqūwa is literally 'non-power' or 'weak-power'.
3
Understand the common term
Look for the phrase 'al-mushtarak' which unifies both subtypes. It defines a quality that inclines the receptive thing towards one side of its receptivity.
This definition applies to both subtypes.
4
Reflect on the basis
The passage mentions that these subtypes are based on the four divisions of quality being distinct in essence. Consider why this matters for the classification.
This connects to the broader ontological framework.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القسم الرابع الكيفيات الاستعدادية . وهي استعداد شديد على أن ينفعل كالممراضية واللين ويسمى اللاقوة أو على أن تقاوم ولا تنفعل كالمصحاحية والصلابة ويسمى القوة، فالمشترك كيفية بها يترجح القابل في أحد جانبي قبوله. ومبناهما على كون الأقسام الأربعة للكيف متباينة بالذات). أي التي من جنس الاستعداد لأنها مفسرة باستعداد شديد على أن ينفعل أي تهيؤ لقبول أثر ما بسهولة أو سرعة وهو وهن طبيعي كالممراضية واللين ويسمى اللاقوة أو على أن تقاوم ولا تنفعل أي تهيؤ للمقاومة وبطء للانفعال كالمصحاحية والصلابة وذلك هو الهيئة التي بها صار الجسم لا يقبل المرض، ويتأبى عن الانغماز ويسمى القوة، فإذا حاولنا ذكر أمر يشمل القسمين، ويخصهما قلنا كيفية بها يترجح القابل في أحد جانبي قبوله.
The fourth division: the dispositional qualities. And they are: a strong disposition to be affected, like sickliness and softness, and it is called weak-power (al-lāqūwa); or to resist and not be affected, like healthiness and hardness, and it is called power (al-qūwa). So the common term (al-mushtarak) is a quality by which the receptive thing is inclined towards one of the two sides of its receptivity. And their basis is that the four divisions of quality are distinct in essence. That is, those which are of the genus of disposition, because they are defined as a strong disposition to be affected, i.e., a readiness to accept some effect easily or quickly, which is a natural weakness like sickliness and softness, and it is called weak-power; or to resist and not be affected, i.e., a readiness for resistance and slowness to be affected, like healthiness and hardness, and that is the condition by which the body becomes not accepting of illness and refuses to be compressed, and it is called power. So if we attempt to mention something that encompasses both divisions and is specific to them, we say: a quality by which the receptive thing is inclined towards one of the two sides of its receptivity.
Source pages p. 389

Commentary
This passage defines the fourth category of quality, the dispositional qualities. It first defines two subtypes: the disposition to be affected (ist'idād li al-infī'āl) and the disposition to resist (ist'idād li al-muqāwama). Each is illustrated with examples: sickliness and softness represent weakness to be affected; healthiness and hardness represent resistance. Then it introduces the common term (al-mushtarak) which covers both: a quality that inclines the receptive thing towards one side of its receptivity. The passage then states that the basis of these two subtypes is that the four divisions of quality are distinct in essence. Finally, it rephrases the common definition to emphasize that it encompasses both subtypes.

Correction: The passage makes clear that both are subtypes of the fourth division (dispositional qualities). They differ in direction (being affected vs. resisting) but share the same genus.

Key Takeaway

Definition and Types of Commonality (Mushtarak)
Al-Taftazani defines mushtarak and enumerates eight types of commonality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of a common term (mushtarak) and the eight types of commonality between things.

Core Idea
Commonality is not just homonymy; it includes species, genus, quantity, quality, and more.

Al-Taftazani begins this footnote by defining the common term (mushtarak) as a single word applied equally to several distinct realities. He then classifies commonality into eight types, each with a specific term, based on the nature of the common property. This classification is essential for precise reasoning in logic and theology.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition of mushtarak
Read the definition and the example of 'ayn to grasp what makes a term mushtarak: one word, multiple distinct realities, equal application.
The example 'ayn covers eye, spring, and sun disk.
2
Learn the first two types: species (mamāthala) and genus (mujānasa)
These are commonalities by essence. Mamāthala is sharing a species; mujānasa is sharing a genus.
Zayd and 'Amr share humanity (species); human and horse share animality (genus).
3
Learn the types by accident: quantity, quality, relation, shape, position, ends
These are commonalities by accident. Each has a specific term: musāwāh (quantity), mushābaha (quality), munāsaba (relation), unnamed for shape, muwāzana (position), mutābaqa (ends).
Note that the type for shape is not explicitly named in the text.
4
Understand the final type: mushākala (resemblance by relation)
This is a separate type called mushākala, which is commonality by relation.
It appears after the main enumeration.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۱) المشترك هو اللفظ الواحد الذي يطلق على أشياء مختلفة بالحد والحقيقة اطلاقاً متساوياً كالعين تطلق على آلة البصر، ينبوع الماء، وقرص الشمس، وهذه مختلفة الحدود والحقائق . راجع) معيار العلم للغزالي ص ٤٦ : ٤٧ . والاشتراك بين الشيئين، إن كان بالنوع يسمى مماثلة كاشتراك بين زيد وعمرو في الإنسانية، فإن كان بالجنس يسمى مجانسة كاشتراك إنسان وفرس في الحيوانية، وإن كان بالعرض، فإن كان في الكم يسمى مساواة، كاشتراك ذارع من خشب، وذراع من ثوب في الطول، وإن كان في الكيف يسمى مشابهة كاشتراك الانسان والحجر في السواد، وإن كان يسمى مناسبة، كاشتراك زيد وعمرو في بنوة بكر، وإن كان بالشكل يسمى كاشتراك الأرض والهواء في الكرية، وإن كان بالوضع المخصوص يسمى موازنة، وهو أن لا يختلف البعد بينهما كسطح كل فلك، وإن كان بالأطراف يسمى مطابقة . (راجع تعريفات الجرجاني). بالمضاف مشاكلة،
(1) The common term (al-mushtarak) is the single word that is applied equally to different things in definition and reality, like 'ayn applied to the instrument of sight, a water spring, and the disk of the sun, and these are different in definitions and realities. (See: Mi'yār al-'Ilm by al-Ghazali, pp. 46-47.) And commonality (al-ishtirāk) between two things: if it is by species, it is called similarity (mamāthala), like the commonality between Zayd and 'Amr in humanity; and if it is by genus, it is called homogeneity (mujānasa), like the commonality of a human and a horse in animality; and if it is by accident, then if it is in quantity, it is called equality (musāwāh), like the commonality between a cubit of wood and a cubit of cloth in length; and if it is in quality, it is called resemblance (mushābaha), like the commonality between a human and a stone in blackness; and if it is [by relation?], it is called suitability (munāsaba), like the commonality between Zayd and 'Amr in being sons of Bakr; and if it is by shape, it is called [like] the commonality of the earth and air in sphericity; and if it is by specific position, it is called balance (muwāzana), which is that the distance between them does not differ, like the surface of each sphere; and if it is by ends, it is called congruence (mutābaqa). (See: Ta'rīfāt al-Jurjānī). By relation, [it is called] resemblance (mushākala).
Source pages p. 389

Commentary
This footnote defines the term 'mushtarak' (common term) and then classifies the types of commonality (ishtirāk) between things. It begins by defining mushtarak as a single word applied equally to different things with different definitions and realities, like the word 'ayn applied to the eye, a spring, and the sun's disk. Then it lists seven types of commonality: by species (called mamāthala), by genus (mujānasa), by accident in quantity (musāwāh), by accident in quality (mushābaha), by relation (munāsaba), by shape (musāwāt? but named after shape), by specific position (muwāzana), and by ends (mutābaqa). It also mentions a type by relationship called mushākala. This classification is standard in logic and helps distinguish different kinds of commonality between things.

Is Strength to Act a Dispositional Quality? Two Proofs from Taftazani
Taftazani presents two proofs that strength to act is not a dispositional quality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Taftazani's two proofs that strength to act is not a distinct dispositional quality, and the basis of his argument against the majority view.

Core Idea
Strength to act reduces to psychic qualities and bodily resistance—not a third type.

In this passage, al-Taftazani tackles a technical debate within the classification of qualities. He argues that the so-called 'strength to act' (al-quwwa 'alā al-fi'l) does not constitute a separate type of dispositional quality; rather, it can be analyzed into two already recognized categories: psychic qualities (al-kayfiyyāt al-nafsāniyya) and the power of resistance (al-quwwa 'alā al-muqāwama). He presents two proofs to demonstrate the impossibility of a third distinct division.

Guided Path
1
Understand the common definition
Read the shared definition of dispositional qualities that applies to the three considered types. Note the phrase 'complete bodily disposition towards something external' as the common ground.
The three divisions likely refer to the two established subtypes (passional and psychic) plus the debated 'strength to act'.
2
Examine the first proof (wrestling)
See how wrestling involves only knowledge (psychic quality) and limb stiffness (power of resistance), leaving no room for a third division of strength to act.
3
Examine the second proof (heat burning)
Analyze how heat's power to burn would cause overlap with passional qualities if considered dispositional, leading to a logical contradiction of being in two genera.
This proof shows the problem of double classification.
4
Consider the majority view
Note that Taftazani says the majority (al-jumhūr) holds that strength to act is included, so these proofs are presented as arguments against that position, not as definitive rulings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مبنى ذلك على أن القوة على الفعل كالقوة على المصارعة غير داخلة في هذا النوع من الكيفيات، والجمهور على أنها داخلة فيه فالأمر المشترك بين الأقسام الثلاثة هو أنها استعداد جسماني كامل نحو أمر من خارج أو مبدأ جسماني به يتم حدوث أمر حادث على أن حدوثه مترجح به واستدل على كون القوة الشديدة على الفعل غير داخلة في هذا النوع بوجهين : - الأول : أن المصارعة مثلا يتعلق بالعلم بتلك الصناعة، والقوة القوية على تلك الأفعال وهما من الكيفيات النفسانية وبصلابة الأعضاء وكونها في خلقتها الطبيعية بحيث يعسر عطفها ونقلها وذلك عائد إلى القوة على المقاومة واللا انفعال فلا يتحقق قسم ثالث. الثاني : أن الحرارة لها قوة شديدة على الإحراق، فلو كانت داخلة في هذا الجنس مع دخولها في الجنس المسمى بالانفعاليات. أعني الراسخ من الكيفيات المحسوسة لزم تقومها بجنسين ودخولها تحت قسمين متقابلين وكلا الوجهين مبني على أن الكيفيات المحسوسة المساة بالانفعاليات أو الانفعالات والكيفيات النفسانية المسماة بالملكة أو الحال والكيفيات المختصة بالكميات
The basis of that is that the strength to act, like the strength to wrestle, is not included in this type of quality, although the majority (al-jumhūr) hold that it is included. So the common matter among the three divisions is that they are a complete bodily disposition towards something external, or a bodily principle by which the occurrence of an occurring thing is completed, such that its occurrence is preponderated by it. And [the author] argued that the strong power to act is not included in this type by two proofs: The first: that wrestling, for example, involves knowledge of that craft and the strong power over those actions, both of which are psychic qualities (al-kayfiyyāt al-nafsāniyya), and involves hardness of limbs and their being in their natural constitution such that it is difficult to bend and move them, and that reduces to the power of resistance and non-affection, so no third division is realized. The second: that heat has a strong power to burn; so if it were included in this genus while also being included in the genus called the passional qualities (al-inf'i'āliyyāt), i.e., the settled of the sensible qualities, then it would necessarily be constituted by two genera and fall under two opposite divisions. And both proofs are based on the fact that the sensible qualities called passional qualities or passions, and the psychic qualities called disposition (malaka) or state (hāl), and the qualities specific to quantities...
Source pages p. 390

Commentary
This passage addresses the debate about whether the strength to act (al-quwwa 'alā al-fi'l) belongs to the category of dispositional qualities. The author states that the basis is that strength to act, like the strength to wrestle, is not included in this type of quality, although the majority (al-jumhūr) hold that it is included. He then gives the common feature among the three divisions (the two subtypes plus strength to act) - but note that he says 'three divisions' which likely includes the two subtypes plus the strength to act? Actually, the text says 'the common matter among the three divisions is that they are a complete bodily disposition' - this suggests that the author is considering the two subtypes and the strength to act as three, but he argues that the strength to act is not truly distinct. Then he presents two proofs (wajhān) that the strong power to act is not included. The first proof: wrestling involves knowledge of the craft and strong power over those actions, which are psychic qualities, and firmness of limbs, which reduces to the power of resistance, so no third division appears. The second proof: heat has a strong power to burn; if it were included in this genus along with its inclusion in the genus called the passional qualities (al-inf'i'āliyyāt), it would require being constituted by two genera and falling under two opposite divisions. Both proofs are based on the idea that the sensible passional qualities and the psychic qualities (habits/states) and the qualities specific to quantities are distinct.

Key Terms
القوة على الفعل
الجمهور
الكيفيات النفسانية
الانفعاليات
القوة على المقاومة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Taftazani's arguments are definitive and universally accepted.
Correction: He merely provides proofs that strength to act is not a distinct dispositional quality, but the majority (jumhur) holds that it is included.

Checkpoint
What is the first proof (الوجه الأول) that strong power to act is not a dispositional quality?
A) Heat's power to burn would cause overlap with passional qualities
B) Wrestling involves psychic qualities and bodily resistance, so no third division
C) The majority does not consider it a dispositional quality
D) It is a sensible quality like color

Key Takeaway
Taftazani argues that strength to act is not a distinct dispositional quality because it can be reduced to psychic qualities and physical resistance, as shown by the wrestling and heat examples.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often rely on your own abilities—strength, skill, knowledge. But Taftazani shows that what you call 'your strength' is actually a combination of knowledge, physical resilience, and ingrained habits. Where in your life have you taken credit for a 'simple strength' that is actually a composite gift from Allah? How will you acknowledge His provision differently today?
?
If strength to act is not a simple quality but reducible to other things, then your sense of self-sufficiency is an illusion. What specific area of weakness have you been trying to overcome by 'sheer willpower'—and how does this lesson expose the need to rely on Allah's gifts of knowledge, resilience, and opportunity rather than an abstract 'strength'?
?
The majority of scholars held that strength to act is a dispositional quality, yet Taftazani argues otherwise with proofs. Are you willing to hold a position based on evidence even when it contradicts what is commonly accepted? Is there a belief you hold just because it's popular, without having examined the proof for yourself? Commit to investigating one such belief this week.

Dispositive Qualities and the Power to Act
Defines two dispositive qualities and introduces the debate on power to act.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition and distinction of dispositive qualities and the debated inclusion of power to act.

Core Idea
Dispositive qualities prepare a body to receive or resist — but power to act may be different.

In this passage, the author delves into the nature of dispositive qualities, distinguishing between readiness to be affected and readiness to resist. He then raises a key point of contention: does the power to act belong to this category? The majority say yes, but he hints at a different view. Let's explore the text.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
The passage is dense, so take your time.
2
Identify the two kinds of dispositive qualities
Look for the two types: readiness to be affected (like softness) and readiness to resist (like hardness). Notice that each is defined by a specific term and example.
These are called 'non-power' and 'power' respectively.
3
Understand the common definition and the debate
Find the sentence that gives a common definition covering both types. Then note the issue about power to act: the author says it is not included, but the majority think it is.
This sets up the rest of the discussion.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومبناهما على كون الأقسام الأربعة للكيف متباينة بالذات. أي التي من جنس الاستعداد لأنها مفسرة باستعداد شديد على أن ينفعل أي تهيؤ لقبول أثر ما بسهولة أو سرعة وهو وهن طبيعي كالممراضية واللين ويسمى اللاقوة أو على أن تقاوم ولا تنفعل أي تهيؤ للمقاومة وبطء للانفعال كالمصحاحية والصلابة وذلك هو الهيئة التي بها صار الجسم لا يقبل المرض، ويتأبى عن الانغماز ويسمى القوة، فإذا حاولنا ذكر أمر يشمل القسمين، ويخصهما قلنا كيفية بها يترجح القابل في أحد جانبي قبوله ومبنى ذلك على أن القوة على الفعل كالقوة على المصارعة غير داخلة في هذا النوع من الكيفيات، والجمهور على أنها داخلة فيه فالأمر المشترك بين الأقسام الثلاثة من هو أنها استعداد جسماني كامل نحو أمر من خارج أو مبدأ جسماني به يتم حدوث أمر حادث على أن حدوثه مترجح به
And their foundation is that the four categories of quality are essentially distinct. That is, those that are of the kind of disposition (isti'dad) because they are explained as a strong readiness to be affected, i.e., a preparedness to accept some effect with ease or speed, and it is a natural weakness like illness-proneness and softness, and it is called al-lā-quwwa (non-power); or to resist and not be affected, i.e., a preparedness for resistance and slowness to be affected, like healthiness and hardness, and that is the disposition by which the body does not accept illness and resists being indented, and it is called al-quwwa (power). If we try to mention something that includes both divisions and is specific to them, we say: a quality by which the recipient is inclined to one of its two sides of reception. And the basis of that is that the power to act, like the power to wrestle, is not included in this type of qualities, and the majority hold that it is included. So the common thing among the three categories is that it is a complete bodily disposition towards something external or a bodily principle by which the occurrence of an event is completed, such that its occurrence is determined by it.

Commentary
This passage defines the two types of dispositive qualities (al-isti'dādiyya): one is readiness to be affected (infi'āl) like softness, the other is readiness to resist (muqāwama) like hardness. It states that the common element of these two is that they are a bodily disposition that determines reception in one of two directions. Then it raises the issue: is the power to act (al-quwwa 'alā al-fi'l) included in this category? The author notes that the majority include it, but he argues against that. This sets up the subsequent arguments.

Two Arguments Against Power to Act as Dispositive Quality
Two arguments proving power to act is not a dispositive quality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two arguments that demonstrate why strong power to act (al-quwwa 'ala al-fi'l) cannot be considered a dispositive quality, and how the essential distinction of quality categories supports these arguments.

Core Idea
Power to act is not a dispositive quality; it reduces to psychic or bodily qualities.

This lesson examines two arguments that seek to prove that strong power to act does not belong to the dispositive qualities. The first argument uses wrestling as an example to show that power involves psychic qualities and bodily resistance. The second argues that if heat's burning power were included in both dispositive and sensible categories, it would belong to two opposite genera. Both arguments rely on the essential distinction between the four categories of qualities.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Let the author's structure come through.
2
Understand each argument separately
Identify the two arguments (wrestling and heat). Notice how each attempts to show that power to act reduces to existing categories, not forming a new one.
The first argument focuses on wrestling, the second on heat.
3
Recognize the underlying principle
Both arguments are built on the premise that the four quality categories are essentially distinct and mutually exclusive. A single thing cannot belong to two opposite categories.
The passage explicitly states this principle after presenting the arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واستدل على كون القوة الشديدة على الفعل غير داخلة في هذا النوع بوجهين: الأول: أن المصارعة مثلا يتعلق بالعلم بتلك الصناعة، والقوة القوية على تلك الأفعال وهما من الكيفيات النفسانية وبصلابة الأعضاء وكونها في خلقتها الطبيعية بحيث يعسر عطفها ونقلها، وذلك عائد إلى القوة على المقاومة واللا انفعال فلا يتحقق قسم ثالث. الثاني: أن الحرارة لها قوة شديدة على الإحراق، فلو كانت داخلة في هذا الجنس مع دخولها في الجنس المسمى بالانفعاليات، أعني الراسخ من الكيفيات المحسوسة لزم تقومها بجنسين ودخولها تحت قسمين متقابلين وكلا الوجهين مبني على أن الكيفيات المحسوسة المسماة بالانفعاليات أو الانفعالات والكيفيات النفسانية المسماة بالملكة أو الحال والكيفيات المختصة بالكميات والكيفيات الاستعدادية أقسام من الكيف متباينة بالذات يمتنع صدق البعض منها على شيء مما صدق عليها الآخر، وإلا فلا يمتنع أن تكون القدرة من حيث اختصاصها بذوات الأنفس من الكيفيات النفسانية والحرارة من حيث كونها مدركة بالحس من المحسوسات وكل منهما من حيث كونها قوة شديدة فاعلة بالسهولة من الكيفيات الاستعدادية. كما ذكروا أن اللون والاستقامة والانحناء ونحو ذلك من المختصة بالكميات مع كونها من المحسوسات.
And he argued for the fact that strong power to act is not included in this type with two proofs: First: wrestling, for example, involves knowledge of that craft and strong power over those actions, and both of these are of the psychic qualities, and (involves) hardness of limbs and their being in their natural constitution such that it is difficult to bend and move them, and that goes back to the power to resist and non-passivity, so no third division is realized. Second: heat has a strong power to burn. If it were included in this genus while being also included in the genus called affections (al-inf'āliyyāt), i.e., the stable among sensible qualities, then it would be constituted by two genera and fall under two opposite divisions. Both proofs are based on the fact that the sensible qualities called affections or passivities, and the psychic qualities called disposition (malaka) or state (ḥāl), and the qualities proper to quantities, and the dispositive qualities are categories of quality essentially distinct; it is impossible for one of them to be true of something of which the other is true. Otherwise, it would not be impossible for power (al-qudra), in terms of its being particular to souls, to be of the psychic qualities, and heat, in terms of its being perceived by sense, to be of the sensibles, and each of them, in terms of its being a strong power acting with ease, to be of the dispositive qualities, just as they mentioned that color, straightness, curvature, and the like are of the qualities proper to quantities while being among the sensibles.
Source pages p. 390

Commentary
This passage presents two arguments (wajhān) against including the power to act (al-quwwa 'alā al-fi'l) in the category of dispositive qualities. The first argument uses wrestling as an example: it involves psychic qualities (knowledge and skill) and bodily hardness which is a form of resistance, so there is no third distinct kind. The second uses heat: if heat's burning power were included in both the dispositive and the sensible (affection) categories, it would belong to two different genera, which is impossible. Both arguments rely on the essential distinction of the four categories: sensible (infi'āliyyāt), psychic (malaka/ḥāl), proper to quantities, and dispositive. They are essentially different; one thing cannot belong to two categories. The passage concludes with a hypothetical: if they were not distinct, then power (from souls) could be psychic, heat could be sensible, and both could also be dispositive, but that is not the case.

الكيفيات المحسوسة
الكيفيات المختصة بالكميات
الكيفيات الاستعدادية
متباينة بالذات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The two arguments are unrelated or independent of each other.
Correction: Both arguments rely on the same principle: the four categories of quality are essentially distinct, so power to act cannot be a separate category.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Strong power to act is not a dispositive quality because it can be reduced to psychic or bodily qualities, and the four quality categories are essentially exclusive.

Definition of Sensibles (al-Hissiyyat)
Defines 'al-ḥissiyyāt' with two technical meanings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two meanings of 'sensibles' in Islamic epistemology and how they relate to sense perception.

Core Idea
Sensibles are not just external sense data—they include inner feelings and broader empirical categories.

In this passage, the commentator clarifies the term 'sensibles' (al-ḥissiyyāt), showing that it goes beyond simple observation to encompass a range of propositions involving sense. This distinction is essential for understanding how theologians classify knowledge. Read the definition and note the two distinct meanings.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its English translation to get an overview of the term 'sensibles'.
Pay attention to the two meanings introduced.
2
Identify the first meaning
The first meaning is propositions known directly through external or internal sense. Notice that these are particular judgments from observation, called maḥsūsāt (external) or wijdāniyyāt (internal).
This covers direct sense perception.
3
Identify the second meaning
The second meaning broadens sensibles to include propositions where sense has some involvement, such as empirical generalizations, recurrent reports, and some intuitive judgments.
This is a wider epistemological category.
4
Compare the two meanings
Reflect on how the first meaning is narrower (direct sense) while the second is broader (sense involvement). Both rely on sense, but in different ways.
5
Address the common misunderstanding
Consider the misconception that sensibles only refer to external sense perception. The passage shows that internal sense and broader categories are also included.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحسيات : جمع الحس. وتسمى المحسوسات وتطلق في القضايا على معنيين الأول هو القضايا التي يجزم بها العقل بمجرد تصور طرفيها بواسطة الحس الظاهر أو الباطن، وهي كلها أحكام جزئية حاصلة من المشاهدات فإذا كانت بواسطة الحس الظاهر سمیت محسوسات، وإذا كانت بواسطة الحس الباطن سميت وجدانيات مثل شعورنا بأن لنا فكرة وإرادة وخوفاً وغضباً . الثاني : ما للحس مدخل فيه فيتناول التجريبيات والمتواترات وأحكام الوهم في المحسوسات وبعض الحدسيات والمشاهدات وبعض الوجدانيات .
al-Ḥissiyyāt: plural of ḥiss (sense). They are also called al-maḥsūsāt (perceived things). In propositions, it is used in two meanings: The first is propositions that the mind affirms by merely conceiving their two extremes through the external or internal sense, and they are all particular judgments obtained from direct observations. If they are through the external sense, they are called maḥsūsāt; if through the internal sense, they are called wijdāniyyāt (inner feelings), like our feeling that we have thought, will, fear, and anger. The second: that in which sense has some involvement, so it includes empirical (tajrībiyyāt), mutawātirāt (recurrent reports), the judgments of the estimative faculty (wahm) concerning sensibles, some intuitive judgments (ḥadsiyyāt), some observations (mushāhadāt), and some inner feelings (wijdāniyyāt).
Source pages p. 391

Commentary
This footnote defines the term 'al-ḥissiyyāt' (sensibles) and its use in propositions. It gives two distinct meanings: first, propositions known directly through external or internal senses, which are particular judgments from observation; second, a broader sense including propositions where sense has some role, such as empirical generalizations, recurrent reports, and certain intuitive judgments.

Classification of Qualities: Multiple Categories
Explanation of predispositional qualities and their cross-categorization with other quality types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a single attribute can belong to multiple categories of quality from different perspectives, based on the example of power and heat.

Core Idea
One attribute can be classified under different quality types depending on perspective.

In kalam and logic, qualities are divided into categories that are essentially distinct. However, a single attribute may fall under multiple categories when considered from different angles. The passage illustrates this with power and heat, showing how they can be both psychological/sensible and predispositional.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on qualities
Read the Arabic passage about predispositional qualities and their distinctness. Then read the translation to understand the key concepts.
Focus on how the author explains essential distinctness.
2
Identify the categories mentioned
Note the three categories: predispositional qualities, psychological qualities, and sensibles. Also note the mention of qualities specific to quantities.
3
Understand cross-categorization
See how power and heat are said to belong to multiple categories from different aspects. This shows that classification depends on the aspect considered.
The example of color being both a quantity-specific attribute and a sensible reinforces this.
4
Reflect on the implications
Think about why this distinction matters: it prevents confusion when attributes are discussed in different contexts.
Connects to the broader theological discourse on divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والكيفيات الاستعدادية أقسام من الكيف متباينة بالذات يمتنع صدق البعض منها على شيء مما صدق عليها الآخر، وإلا فلا يمتنع أن تكون القدرة من حيث اختصاصها بذوات الأنفس من الكيفيات النفسانية والحرارة من حيث كونها مدركة بالحس من المحسوسات وكل منهما من حيث كونها قوة شديدة فاعلة بالسهولة من الكيفيات الاستعدادية. كما ذكروا أن اللون والاستقامة والانحناء ونحو ذلك من المختصة بالكميات مع كونها من المحسوسات (۱) . (۱) الحسيات : جمع الحس. وتسمى المحسوسات وتطلق في القضايا على معنيين الأول هو القضايا التي يجزم بها العقل بمجرد تصور طرفيها بواسطة الحس الظاهر أو الباطن، وهي كلها أحكام جزئية حاصلة من المشاهدات فإذا كانت بواسطة الحس الظاهر سمیت محسوسات، وإذا كانت بواسطة الحس الباطن سميت وجدانيات مثل شعورنا بأن لنا فكرة وإرادة وخوفاً وغضباً . الثاني : ما للحس مدخل فيه فيتناول التجريبيات والمتواترات وأحكام الوهم في المحسوسات وبعض الحدسيات والمشاهدات وبعض الوجدانيات .
And predispositional qualities (al-kayfiyyāt al-istiʿdādiyya) are divisions of quality (al-kayf) that are essentially distinct (mutabāyina bi-l-dhāt), such that it is impossible for some of them to be true of something of which the others are true. Otherwise, it is not impossible that power (al-qudra), in terms of its being specific to souls, be of the psychological qualities (al-kayfiyyāt al-nafsāniyya), and heat (al-ḥarāra), in terms of its being perceived by sense, be of the sensibles (al-maḥsūsāt), and each of them, in terms of being a strong potency acting with ease, be of the predispositional qualities (al-kayfiyyāt al-istiʿdādiyya). As they mentioned that color, straightness, curvature, and the like are specific to quantities (al-kammiyyāt) while being among the sensibles (al-maḥsūsāt). (1) (1) Sensory propositions (al-ḥissiyyāt): plural of sense (al-ḥiss). They are called sensibles (al-maḥsūsāt), and in propositions (al-qaḍāyā) they are used in two senses. The first: propositions that the mind affirms with certainty merely by conceiving their two terms through the external or internal sense, and they are all particular judgments (aḥkām juzʾiyya) obtained from observations (al-mushāhadāt). If they are through the external sense, they are called sensibles (maḥsūsāt); if through the internal sense, they are called introspections (wijdāniyyāt), such as our awareness that we have thought, will, fear, and anger. The second: that in which sense has a role, so it includes experimental judgments (al-tajribiyyāt), recurrent reports (al-mutawātirāt), the judgments of estimation (awhām) concerning sensibles, some intuitive judgments (al-ḥadsiyyāt), observations (al-mushāhadāt), and some introspections (al-wijdāniyyāt).
Source pages p. 391

Commentary
This passage comprises two parts. The first part is a classification of qualities, specifically explaining that predispositional qualities (الكيفيات الاستعدادية) are a type of quality that are essentially distinct from other types; they cannot simultaneously apply to the same object with other distinct types. However, it is possible for a single attribute to belong to multiple categories from different perspectives. For example, power (القدرة) is specific to souls, so in that respect it is a psychological quality; heat (الحرارة) is perceived by the senses, so it is a sensible; but both power and heat can also be considered predispositional qualities because they are strong potencies that act with ease. The text further notes that attributes like color, straightness, and curvature are specific to quantities (الكميات) while also being sensibles. This illustrates the multiple categorization of qualities based on different aspects.

The second part is a footnote defining الحسيات (sensory propositions). The term is explained to have two senses in logic. The first sense: propositions that the mind affirms solely upon conceiving their two terms through sense—either external sense (then called محسوسات, i.e., sensibles) or internal sense (then called وجدانيات, i.e., introspections). These are particular judgments derived from direct observation, like awareness of one's own thoughts, will, fear, or anger. The second sense is broader: it includes any proposition in which sense plays a role, thus encompassing experimental judgments (التجريبيات), recurrent reports (المتواترات), the judgments of estimation (أحكام الوهم) about sensibles, and some intuitive judgments (الحدسيات), observations (المشاهدات), and introspections (الوجدانيات). The footnote clarifies the terminological range of sensory evidence in kalam and logic.

Key Terms
الكيفيات الاستعدادية
الكيفيات النفسانية
المحسوسات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: An attribute can only belong to one category of quality.
Correction: The passage shows that attributes can be classified under multiple categories when considered from different perspectives.

Checkpoint

B) Because the classification depends on the aspect considered.
C) Because power is not a real attribute.
D) Because power can only be psychological.

Key Takeaway
A single attribute can belong to multiple quality categories when considered from different perspectives, so classification is aspect-dependent.

Definition of Sensory Propositions (al-Ḥissiyyāt)
Definition of al-ḥissiyyāt in two senses: narrow (sense alone) and broad (sense involved).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two meanings of 'al-ḥissiyyāt' (sensory propositions) in logic: propositions known solely by sense, and propositions in which sense plays a role.

Core Idea
Sensory propositions have a narrow and a broad meaning in logic.

In logical and theological discourse, the term 'sensory propositions' (al-ḥissiyyāt) is used in two distinct senses. The first refers to direct sense-based judgments (sensibles and introspections), while the second encompasses any proposition where sense has a role, including experimental, recurrent, and intuitive judgments. This passage clarifies the range of sensory evidence.

Guided Path
1
Read the footnote on sensory propositions
Read the Arabic text defining al-ḥissiyyāt and its two meanings. Then read the translation carefully.
Pay attention to the distinction between narrow and broad sense.
2
Identify the first meaning
The first meaning: propositions affirmed by the mind merely by conceiving their terms through external or internal sense. These are particular judgments (observations). External sense gives 'sensibles', internal sense gives 'introspections'.
Examples: seeing a color (external), feeling fear (internal).
3
Identify the second meaning
The second meaning is broader: any proposition in which sense has a role. This includes experimental judgments, recurrent reports, judgments of estimation about sensibles, some intuitive judgments, observations, and some introspections.
Here sense is not the sole source but plays a part.
4
Compare the two meanings
Notice the difference: first meaning requires sense alone; second meaning only requires sense to be involved. The second is more inclusive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والكيفيات الاستعدادية أقسام من الكيف متباينة بالذات يمتنع صدق البعض منها على شيء مما صدق عليها الآخر، وإلا فلا يمتنع أن تكون القدرة من حيث اختصاصها بذوات الأنفس من الكيفيات النفسانية والحرارة من حيث كونها مدركة بالحس من المحسوسات وكل منهما من حيث كونها قوة شديدة فاعلة بالسهولة من الكيفيات الاستعدادية. كما ذكروا أن اللون والاستقامة والانحناء ونحو ذلك من المختصة بالكميات مع كونها من المحسوسات (۱) . (۱) الحسيات : جمع الحس. وتسمى المحسوسات وتطلق في القضايا على معنيين الأول هو القضايا التي يجزم بها العقل بمجرد تصور طرفيها بواسطة الحس الظاهر أو الباطن، وهي كلها أحكام جزئية حاصلة من المشاهدات فإذا كانت بواسطة الحس الظاهر سمیت محسوسات، وإذا كانت بواسطة الحس الباطن سميت وجدانيات مثل شعورنا بأن لنا فكرة وإرادة وخوفاً وغضباً . الثاني : ما للحس مدخل فيه فيتناول التجريبيات والمتواترات وأحكام الوهم في المحسوسات وبعض الحدسيات والمشاهدات وبعض الوجدانيات .
And predispositional qualities (al-kayfiyyāt al-istiʿdādiyya) are divisions of quality (al-kayf) that are essentially distinct (mutabāyina bi-l-dhāt), such that it is impossible for some of them to be true of something of which the others are true. Otherwise, it is not impossible that power (al-qudra), in terms of its being specific to souls, be of the psychological qualities (al-kayfiyyāt al-nafsāniyya), and heat (al-ḥarāra), in terms of its being perceived by sense, be of the sensibles (al-maḥsūsāt), and each of them, in terms of being a strong potency acting with ease, be of the predispositional qualities (al-kayfiyyāt al-istiʿdādiyya). As they mentioned that color, straightness, curvature, and the like are specific to quantities (al-kammiyyāt) while being among the sensibles (al-maḥsūsāt). (1) (1) Sensory propositions (al-ḥissiyyāt): plural of sense (al-ḥiss). They are called sensibles (al-maḥsūsāt), and in propositions (al-qaḍāyā) they are used in two senses. The first: propositions that the mind affirms with certainty merely by conceiving their two terms through the external or internal sense, and they are all particular judgments (aḥkām juzʾiyya) obtained from observations (al-mushāhadāt). If they are through the external sense, they are called sensibles (maḥsūsāt); if through the internal sense, they are called introspections (wijdāniyyāt), such as our awareness that we have thought, will, fear, and anger. The second: that in which sense has a role, so it includes experimental judgments (al-tajribiyyāt), recurrent reports (al-mutawātirāt), the judgments of estimation (awhām) concerning sensibles, some intuitive judgments (al-ḥadsiyyāt), observations (al-mushāhadāt), and some introspections (al-wijdāniyyāt).
Source pages p. 391

Commentary
This passage comprises two parts. The first part is a classification of qualities, specifically explaining that predispositional qualities (الكيفيات الاستعدادية) are a type of quality that are essentially distinct from other types; they cannot simultaneously apply to the same object with other distinct types. However, it is possible for a single attribute to belong to multiple categories from different perspectives. For example, power (القدرة) is specific to souls, so in that respect it is a psychological quality; heat (الحرارة) is perceived by the senses, so it is a sensible; but both power and heat can also be considered predispositional qualities because they are strong potencies that act with ease. The text further notes that attributes like color, straightness, and curvature are specific to quantities (الكميات) while also being sensibles. This illustrates the multiple categorization of qualities based on different aspects.

The second part is a footnote defining الحسيات (sensory propositions). The term is explained to have two senses in logic. The first sense: propositions that the mind affirms solely upon conceiving their two terms through sense—either external sense (then called محسوسات, i.e., sensibles) or internal sense (then called وجدانيات, i.e., introspections). These are particular judgments derived from direct observation, like awareness of one's own thoughts, will, fear, or anger. The second sense is broader: it includes any proposition in which sense plays a role, thus encompassing experimental judgments (التجريبيات), recurrent reports (المتواترات), the judgments of estimation (أحكام الوهم) about sensibles, and some intuitive judgments (الحدسيات), observations (المشاهدات), and introspections (الوجدانيات). The footnote clarifies the terminological range of sensory evidence in kalam and logic.

Key Takeaway
Sensory propositions (al-ḥissiyyāt) have a narrow definition (based solely on sense) and a broad definition (where sense plays a role, including many types of judgment).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you feel a strong emotion like anger or fear, do you treat that inner experience as reliable knowledge? Have you ever acted on an emotion and later discovered it deceived you? What would it take to test your inner senses against external evidence, as this lesson distinguishes?
?
This lesson shows that even indirect reports (mutawātirāt) are classified as sensory propositions. How many of your deeply held beliefs are based on such indirect sensory evidence rather than direct observation? Would you be willing to trace one belief back to its sensory root and examine whether it truly falls under the narrow or broad meaning?
?
You rely on tajribiyyāt every day—like knowing that fire burns or that ice melts. But have you ever considered that these 'certainties' are only probable? If you were required to prove the reliability of repeated experience using only logic, could you? What does that do to your confidence in everyday knowledge?

Chapter Four: On the 'Where' and Two Investigations
Sanusi introduces the fourth chapter on spatial location and states two necessary propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two foundational propositions that open the chapter on spatial location (al-ayn) and their role as necessary truths in kalam.

Core Idea
Space exists necessarily, and motion permeates the moving body.

This chapter begins a new investigation into the nature of spatial location, a topic that connects physics, metaphysics, and theology. Saʿd al-Dīn al-Taftāzānī lays down two preliminary propositions — the necessary existence of space and the movement of the interior of a moving body — that will serve as the foundation for subsequent discussions. These propositions are presented as necessary (ḍarūrī), meaning they are self-evident truths that do not require proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the chapter heading and two propositions
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note that this is not an argument but an announcement of what the chapter contains.
The chapter is about 'al-ayn' (where/spatial location), not 'al-kawn' (being).
2
Understand the first proposition: existence of space is necessary
Reflect on what it means for something to be necessary in kalam. This proposition asserts that space cannot be denied; it is a fundamental category.
This is a starting point for discussions about void, place, and body.
3
Understand the second proposition: interior of moving body moves
Consider why this proposition might be needed. It connects motion with the internal parts of a body, implying that motion is not merely superficial.
This relates to theories of motion and rest in kalam.
4
Reflect on the significance of these propositions
These are taken as necessary truths. Ask yourself: why would the author begin with these? How do they set up the rest of the chapter?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الرابع في الأين وفيه مبحثان ۱ - الكون وجوده ضروري ۲ - الباطن من أجزاء الجسم المتحرك متحرك .
Chapter Four: On the 'Where' and it contains two investigations. 1 - The existence of space (al-kawn) is necessary. 2 - The interior of the parts of a moving body is moving.
Source pages p. 393

Commentary
This passage is a chapter heading and two numbered propositions. It sets out the fourth chapter of the work, which deals with the concept of spatial location (al-ayn). The chapter contains two investigations (mabḥathān). The first proposition states that the existence of space (al-kawn) is necessary (ḍarūrī), meaning that it is a self-evident or necessary truth. The second proposition states that the interior (al-bāṭin) of the parts of a moving body (al-jism al-mutaḥarrik) is itself moving. This likely relates to the nature of motion and spatial extension within a body. The passage is brief and functions as an outline.

The Four Types of Kawn According to the Mutakallimun
Taftazani defines kawn, its four types, and answers an objection about haraka.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the mutakallimun define kawn (being in a place) and derive its four types: ijtima', iftiraq, haraka, and sukun, including the nuance of possibility in separation and the objection about motion.

Core Idea
Kawn is a necessary accident with four types: conjunction, separation, motion, and rest.

This passage from Taftazani's Sharh al-Maqasid lays out the foundational kalam concept of 'kawn' (being in a place). The mutakallimun treat kawn as a real accident with necessary existence, despite denying other relational accidents. Here, Taftazani defines kawn and classifies its four types based on the substance's relation to another substance and to its own previous location. He also addresses a subtle objection about the definition of motion, clarifying that leaving a location is identical to entering another under different relations.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of kawn
Read the opening lines where kawn is defined as 'occurrence of a substance in a location' and noted as having necessary existence. Notice that the mutakallimun affirm its existence while denying other relative accidents.
The mutakallimun consider kawn a real accident, unlike other relational accidents they reject.
2
Understand the classification criteria
Taftazani classifies kawn based on two binary considerations: whether the substance's location is considered in relation to another substance or not, and if so, whether a third can interpose; if not, whether the occurrence is preceded by occurrence in another location or the same.
These criteria yield four mutually exclusive types.
3
Study the four types
Work through the four types: ijtimāʿ (conjunction) and iftirāq (separation) when relative to another substance; ḥaraka (motion) and sukūn (rest) when not relative. Note the distinction between possibility and actual interposition in separation.
Separation considers possibility (imkan) of a third interposing, not actual existence, to include void.
4
Examine the objection and answer
Taftazani presents an objection: if motion is defined as preceded by occurrence in another location, then leaving the first location would not be motion. The answer, following al-Amidi, is that leaving the first location is identical to entering the second under different relations.
Motion is defined relationally: leaving the first is the same event as entering the second.
5
Reflect on the conceptual nuance
Consider why the mutakallimun consider possibility rather than actuality for interposition, and how they handle the priority of location and occurrence in motion and rest.
The priority can be estimated, e.g., an eternally resting body has estimated priority of location.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الأول: الكون وجوده ضروري وأنواعه أربعة لأن حصول الجوهر في الحيز إن اعتبر بالنسبة إلى جوهر آخر فإن أمكن تخلل ثالث بينهما، فافتراق على تفاوت أقسامه في القرب والبعد، وإلا فاجتماع، ومن أسمائه المجاورة والمماسة على الأقرب. وهو النسبة إلى المكان أعني كون الشيء في الحيز، وللقوم في تحقيق مباحثه طريقان: أحدهما للمتكلمين، والآخر للفلاسفة وللقدماء من المتكلمين في طريقهم شعب وتفاريع قليلة الجدوى، لا نطول الكتاب بذكرها، بل نقتصر على ما بهما. فنقول: المتكلمون يعبرون عن الأين أعني حصول الجوهر في الحيز بالكون ويعترفون بوجوده وإن أنكروا وجود سائر الأعراض النسبية، وقد حصروه في أربعة أنواع هي الاجتماع والافتراق والحركة والسكون، لأن حصول الجوهر في الحيز إما أن يعتبر بالنسبة إلى جوهر آخر أو لا، وعلى الأول إما أن يكون بحيث يمكن أن يتوسطهما ثالث فهو الافتراق وإلا فالاجتماع، واعتبر مكان تخلل الثالث دون تحققه ليشمل افتراق الجوهرين بتخلل الخلاء، فإنه لا ثالث بينهما بالفعل بل بالإمكان. وعلى الثاني: إن كان مسبوقاً بحصوله في حيز آخر فهو الحركة، وإن كان مسبوقاً بحصوله في ذلك الحيز فالسكون، فيكون السكون حصولاً ثانياً في حيز أول، والحركة حصولاً أول في حيز ثان، وأولية الحيز في السكون قد لا يكون تحقيقاً بل تقديراً كما في الساكن الذي لا يتحرك قطعاً، فلا يحصل في حيز ثان، وكذا أولية الحصول في الحركة لجواز أن ينعدم المتحرك في آن انقطاع الحركة، فلا يتحقق له حصول ثان. فإن قيل: إذا اعتبر في الحركة المسبوقية بالحصول في حيز آخر لم يكن الخروج من الحيز الأول حركة مع أنه حركة وفاقاً. قلنا: إنما يلزم ذلك لو لم يكن الخروج من الحيز الأول نفس الحصول الأول في الحيز الثاني على ما صرح به الآمدي وتحقيقه، أن الحصول الأول في الحيز الثاني من حيث الإضافة إليه دخول وحركة إليه، ومن حيث
The first investigation: The 'kawn' (being in a place) has necessary existence and its types are four, because the occurrence of a substance in a location, if considered in relation to another substance, then if a third can interpose between them, it is separation (iftiraq) with variation in its degrees of proximity and distance, otherwise it is conjunction (ijtima'), and among its names are adjacency and contact, on the closest view. And it is the relation to place, meaning the thing's being in the location. And for the people there are two paths in investigating its topics: one for the mutakallimun, and the other for the philosophers. And for the early mutakallimun in their path there are branches and subdivisions of little benefit; we will not lengthen the book by mentioning them, but we restrict ourselves to what suffices. So we say: The mutakallimun express 'al-ayn' (location), meaning the occurrence of a substance in a location, by 'kawn' and they affirm its existence even though they deny the existence of other relative accidents. And they have confined it to four types: conjunction (ijtima'), separation (iftiraq), motion (haraka), and rest (sukun). Because the occurrence of a substance in a location: either it is considered in relation to another substance or not. On the first [case], either it is such that a third can interpose between them, then it is separation; otherwise it is conjunction. And they considered the possibility of interposition of a third without its actual existence to include the separation of two substances by the interposition of void, for there is no third between them actually but only potentially. On the second [case]: if it is preceded by its occurrence in another location, it is motion; and if it is preceded by its occurrence in that same location, it is rest. So rest is a second occurrence in a first location, and motion is a first occurrence in a second location. And the priority of the location in rest may not be actual but estimated, as in something at rest that never moves, so it does not occur in a second location. Likewise, the priority of occurrence in motion is due to the possibility that the moving thing ceases to exist at the moment the motion stops, so it has no second occurrence. If it is said: If motion is considered to be preceded by occurrence in another location, then leaving the first location would not be motion, whereas it is motion by agreement. We say: That would only follow if leaving the first location were not identical with the first occurrence in the second location, as al-Amidi stated and its verification: that the first occurrence in the second location, with respect to relation to it, is entrance and motion toward it, and with respect to
Source pages p. 395

Commentary
This passage defines the concept of 'kawn' (being in a place) as understood by the mutakallimun, who consider it a real accident with necessary existence, unlike other relative accidents they deny. The passage proceeds as follows: First, it establishes that 'kawn' has necessary existence and divides into four types. The division is based on whether the substance's location is considered in relation to another substance or not. If in relation to another substance, then if a third substance can interpose between them, it is 'iftiraq' (separation); otherwise it is 'ijtima'' (conjunction). The author notes that the possibility (imkan) of interposition is considered, not actual existence, to account for separation across void. If not in relation to another substance, then the substance's current occurrence is either preceded by occurrence in another location (motion, haraka) or by occurrence in the same location (rest, sukun). Rest is defined as a second occurrence in the same location (i.e., persistence), while motion is a first occurrence in a new location. The author addresses subtle issues: the 'priority' of the location in rest may be estimated rather than actual (e.g., an eternally resting body), and the 'priority' of occurrence in motion is based on the possibility that the moving thing ceases to exist at the moment motion stops, so no second occurrence exists. Then an objection is raised: if motion is defined as preceded by occurrence in another location, then the act of leaving the first location would not be motion, yet it is motion by consensus. The answer, following al-Amidi, states that leaving the first location is identical to the first occurrence in the second location; the substance's departure from the first location is the same event as its entry into the second, but considered under different relations—thus it is motion. The passage ends mid-sentence (apparently from the page break).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'possibility of interposition' means there is actually a third substance between two others, but the text clarifies it is only possibility (imkan) to include cases of separation through void where no actual third exists.
Correction: The mutakallimun consider the possibility (imkan) of a third interposing, not its actual existence, to account for substances separated by void.

Checkpoint
According to the mutakallimun as presented in the passage, what distinguishes 'motion' (haraka) from 'rest' (sukun)?
A) Motion involves a relation to another substance, while rest does not.
B) Motion is preceded by occurrence in another location, while rest is not preceded by any occurrence.
C) Motion requires actual interposition of a third substance, while rest does not.
D) Motion is a first occurrence in a second location, while rest is a second occurrence in the same location.

Key Takeaway
The mutakallimun classify kawn into four types based on relational and temporal considerations, with motion defined as first occurrence in a new location and rest as second occurrence in the same location.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You are constantly in motion or rest—how does the mutakallimun's definition remind you that every moment of your movement is a new occurrence in a location, a new creation by Allah? What would it mean for you to truly recognize that each step you take is a first occurrence in that place, not a continuation of the past?
?
If rest is a second occurrence in the same location, then your stillness is also a divine act of placing you there. How does this understanding change your awareness of Allah's constant preservation of your existence in every moment, even when you are not moving? Will you consciously affirm this with your heart the next time you sit or stand?
?
The passage distinguishes between actual and possible interposition to account for void. Do you ever consider the 'void'—the unseen possibilities—in your own life? How can trusting Allah's absolute knowledge and power over all possibilities (including the void) free you from anxiety about what might happen?
